⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان الدسوقي المنطقِ المفيدِ لِتَصوُّره ، وقوله : ( وموضوعه) عطف على الحاجة أيضاً، وعلى متعلّق بيان ما يفيدُ التَّصديق بموضوعيَّة موضوع المنطق، وبهذا ظهرَ لَكَ أنَّ الاشتمال مِنَ اشتمالِ الكُلِّ على أجزائِهِ، وأنَّ البيانَ مُستعمل فيما شاع فيه من ذكرٍ ما يفيد التصديق بالنسبة للحاجة والموضوع، وفي غيرِهِ مِن ذكرِ مَا يفيدُ التَّصوَّرَ بالنسبة للتعريف . (قَوْلُهُ : وَسَتَعْرِفُ . . . إِلَخ) أي : وهذه الأمورُ الثَّلاثةُ الَّتي اشتملت عليها المقدّمة يتوقَّفُ عليها الشروع في مسائلِ العِلْمِ ، وستعرفُ ... إلخ)، وحينئذ ؛ يكون المراد بالمقدمة هنا ما ذكره . (قَوْلُهُ: وَلَمَّا كَانَ... إلخ) جواب عَمَّا يُقالُ : المقدمة معقودة لبيان الحاجة والتَّعريف والموضوع، فلأي شيءٍ ذكر فيها تقسيم العِلْمِ وقدَّمَهُ؟ . وحاصل الجواب : أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ الَّذي هو من جملة ما يتوفَّفُ عليه الشروع؛ يتوقَّفُ على التقسيم، فيكونُ الشُّروعُ مُتوقفاً عليه أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ المتوقفَ على المتوقفِ على شيءٍ؛ مُتوقِّفٌ على ذلكَ الشَّيء، فَلِذَا ذكرَهُ، وإنَّما قدَّمَهُ على تلك الأمور؛ لأنَّ بيان الحاجةِ مُتوقّفٌ عليه، وبيانُ الحاجة يؤدّي إلى التّعريف، وبيانُ التَّعريف مُقدَّم على الموضوعِ، فَلَزِمَ مِن ذلك تقديمُهُ على جميعِهَا ، فَلِذَا قدَّمَهُ عليها . فإنْ قُلْتَ: بيانُ الحاجة لا يتوقَّفُ على تقسيم العِلْمِ إِلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ، بل يكفي أنْ يُقال: العِلْمُ إِمَّا ضروري أو نظري، والنظريُّ قد يقع فيه الخطأ، فاحتيج إلى قانون يعصم الفكر عن الخطأ فيه ؛ وهو المنطقُ ، والجواب : إنَّ المراد ببيانِ الحاجة؛ بيانها على وجه يشعر بالاحتياج إلى قِسْمي المنطق وهما : الموصل إلى التّصورِ، والموصل إلى التصديق، فاحتيج حينئذ إلى تقسيم العِلْمِ إلى تصورٍ وإلى تصديق؛ إذْ لو لم يقسم العِلْم أوّلاً لهما، ولم يبيّن أنَّ في كلِّ منهما ضَروريًا ونظريا يمكن اكتسابُهُ من الضَّروريّ؛ لجَازَ أنْ تكونَ التَّصوّرات كلّها ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجةً إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصوُّرِ ، وأن تكونَ التَّصديقات كلُّهَا ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجة إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصديق، فلم يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق، وقد علمتَ أنَّ المراد ببيان الحاجة ما ذكر، وبالتقسيم المذكور تجد المقصود المذكور، وَلَمَّا كانَ التَّقسيم إلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ أوَّليًّا، والتَّقسيم إلى الضروري والنظري ثانويا ؛ قدَّمَ ذاكَ على هذا .
When it was [said] by al-Dasuqi: 'the logic useful for its conception', and his saying: 'and its subject' is a conjunct to the need as well, and to the dependent of the explanation of what benefits assent regarding the subjecthood of the subject of logic. By this it became clear to you that the inclusion is of the inclusion of the whole in its parts, and that 'explanation' is used in what is common in it of mentioning what benefits assent regarding the need and subject, and in other than that of mentioning what benefits conception regarding the definition. (His saying: 'And you will know... etc.') meaning: these three matters that the introduction comprises, the commencement in the issues of the science depends on each of them, and you will know... etc. So then the intended meaning of 'introduction' here is what he mentioned. (His saying: 'And when it was... etc.') is an answer to what is said: 'The introduction is dedicated to explaining the need, definition, and subject; so for what reason is the division of knowledge mentioned in it and presented before them?' The summary of the answer: that the explanation of the need, which is among the things on which the commencement depends, itself depends on the division, so the commencement also depends on it; because whatever depends on something that depends on a thing depends on that thing. Therefore he mentioned it. And he only presented it before those matters because the explanation of the need depends on it, and the explanation of the need leads to the definition, and the explanation of the definition is prior to the subject, so from that its presentation before all of them follows. Therefore he presented it before them. If you say: 'The explanation of the need does not depend on dividing knowledge into conception and assent; rather it suffices to say: knowledge is either necessary or speculative, and speculation may contain error, so there is need for a canon that protects the mind from error in it, and that is logic.' And the answer: that what is intended by 'explanation of the need' is its explanation in a manner that indicates the need for the two parts of logic, which are: that which leads to conception and that which leads to assent. So then there is need for dividing knowledge into conception and assent; because if knowledge were not first divided into these two, and it were not clarified that in each of them there is necessary and speculative that can be acquired from the necessary, then it would be possible that all conceptions are necessary, and so there is no need for the topics of that which leads to conception, and that all assents are necessary, and so there is no need for the topics of that which leads to assent. Thus the need for the two parts of logic is not established. And you have known that what is intended by 'explanation of the need' is what is mentioned, and by the mentioned division you will find the mentioned purpose. And since the division into conception and assent is primary, and the division into necessary and speculative is secondary, he presented the former before the latter.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the structure of the introduction to a logic text. The commentator explains why the author included the division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) in the introduction, and why it is presented before the explanation of the need (haja), definition (ta'rif), and subject (mawdu'). The key point is that the explanation of the need for logic itself depends on this division. To show that logic is needed for both conception and assent, one must first establish that knowledge comprises these two types, and that each contains speculative (nazari) elements that require a canon. If only the division into necessary and speculative were given, it would not prove the need for both parts of logic (that which leads to conception and that which leads to assent). Therefore, the author places the dichotomy of tasawwur/tasdiq first. The commentator also addresses an objection that a simpler division suffices, and answers by clarifying the precise purpose of the need explanation. This passage is foundational for understanding the orientation of the entire logic text.

بيان الحاجة
تقسيم العلم
تصور
تصديق

Checkpoint
Why does the author present the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq before the explanation of the need in the introduction?
A) Because it is a more general classification than necessary and speculative.
B) Because the explanation of the need for both parts of logic depends on this division.
C) To follow the order of the matn without reason.
D) Because the division into tasawwur and tasdiq is a standard topic in logic.

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq is placed first because it is a prerequisite for demonstrating the need for both parts of logic, which in turn justifies the definition and subject.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
Defining Logic Through Its Purpose: Bayān al-Ḥājah and Rasm
Commentary on bayān al-ḥājah and its relation to definition by rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how bayān al-ḥājah leads to defining logic by rasm and distinguish between entailment and direct deduction.

Core Idea
The need for logic itself defines it: an instrument that safeguards the mind from error.

The commentators explain that the very need for logic (bayān al-ḥājah) drives us to define logic by its purpose—a description (rasm) rather than an essential definition (ḥadd). Understanding this transition is key to grasping why logic is introduced the way it is in Islamic textbooks. You will see how the argument for necessity points to the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of bayān al-ḥājah
Bayān al-ḥājah is the explanation of why logic is needed. It argues that without logic, the mind is prone to error in reasoning. This purpose—preventing error—becomes the defining feature of logic.
It is not a definition of logic's essence but of its function.
2
See how the purpose yields a definition by rasm
The commentators note that 'al-munsāq' (leading to) indicates that the explanation of need necessarily leads to a definition of logic as 'an instrument that safeguards the mind from error.' This is a rasm because it describes logic by its property or purpose, not by genus and differentia.
Rasm is acceptable when the essence (māhiyya) is unknown or controversial.
3
Differentiate entailment from deduction
Attar and Dasuqi clarify that bayān al-ḥājah entails the definition (luzūm) but does not directly produce it. The entailment is obvious (insiyāq) and does not require effort (yasūqu). The definition is not 'deduced' from the need but is a necessary concomitant.
This subtle point prevents confusion: the need argument implies the definition but is not a formal proof of it.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider how usūl al-fiqh defines qiyās (analogy) by its purpose: 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause ('illah).' This is a rasm, as it describes qiyās by its function, not by a full genus-differentia definition.
Many Islamic sciences define their key tools by purpose rather than essence.

Source Text

مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن
بيانُ الحاجة المنساقُ إلى تعريف المنطقِ
الدسوقي
تصديق ؛ إذْ لو لم يقسم العِلْم أوّلاً لهما، ولم يبيّن أنَّ في كلِّ منهما ضَروريًا ونظريا يمكن اكتسابُهُ من الضَّروريّ؛ لجَازَ أنْ تكونَ التَّصوّرات كلّها ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجةً إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصوُّرِ ، وأن تكونَ التَّصديقات كلُّهَا ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجة إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصديق، فلم يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق، وقد علمتَ أنَّ المراد ببيان الحاجة ما ذكر، وبالتقسيم المذكور تجد المقصود المذكور، وَلَمَّا كانَ التَّقسيم إلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ أوَّليًّا، والتَّقسيم إلى الضروري والنظري ثانويا ؛ قدَّمَ ذاكَ على هذا . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُنْسَاقُ) صفةٌ لبيان ؛ أي: المؤدّي إلى تعريف عِلْم المنطقِ بالرَّسمِ؛ لأنَّ بيان الحاجةِ يستلزم تعريفَهُ بالرَّسم لا بالحدِّ؛ لأنَّ فائدتَهُ عصمتُه الفكر عن الخطأ، وهذا يستلزم تعريفه، وهو آلة قانونية تعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكرِ كَمَا سبق .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: يَنْسَاقُ) أي : يستلزم، وإنَّما عبر بالانسياق؛ إشارة إلى ظهورِ اللُّزومِ، بِخلافِ مَا لو عبَّرَ بِيسوقُ ؛ فربَّما يتوهَّمُ المعاناة، ففي اختيار الانسياق إشارة إلى أَنَّ استلزامَهُ إِيَّاهُ غيرُ مدخل لتحرير المصنفِ ، أفاده عبد الحكيم، ويعني بذلك التحرير قول صاحبِ الشَّمسيَّةِ : العِلْمُ إِمَّا تَصوُّرٌ، وإِمَّا تَصورٌ معه حكم، إلى قوله : فمسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يعصم عنه، وهو المنطقُ ، وقد اختصرها المصنِّفُ هنا ؛ فَآخِرُ مَا ينساقُ إليه بيان الحاجة ؛ أَنَّهُ مَسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يفيد عصمة الذهنِ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وهو لازِم محمولٌ مُساءٍ للمنطق، ولذا ؛ قال المصنِّفُ : وهو المنطقُ . فثبتَ أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ مُتضمِّنُ لتعريف المنطقِ برسمِهِ، وأمَّا التَّعريفُ ؛ فلا يستلزم ببيان الحاجة؛ لجواز أن يكونَ الرَّسم بشيءٍ آخر دونَ غايتِهِ، لا يُقالُ: إِنَّ بيان الحاجة مِن قبيل التّصديقِ والرَّسم تصوُّرُ ؛ فكيف يتوصَّلُ للتَّصورِ بالتصديقِ، مع أنَّ الواقع العكس؟ والجواب : أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ ينتهي إلى الرسم ويستلزمه، ولا يلزمُ من ذلك أن يكونَ مُوصلاً له، فهذا استلزام لا استنتاج
موقوفاً على تقسيم العِلْمِ إلى قسميهِ؛ شَرَعَ في التقسيم فقال :
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : مَوْقُوْفًا) أي : مُتوقفاً
(قَوْلُهُ : شرع في التقسيم) إظهار في محلّ الإضمار .
العطار
و
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى تَقْسِيم (الْعِلْم لا يُقال: إن بيان الحاجة لا يتوقف على جميع هذه المقدِّماتِ، بل يكفي أنْ يُقَالَ: العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري ... إلخ، ما ذكرَهُ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : المقصود بيان الاحتياج إلى المنطق بقسميه، فلو لم يقسم العلم أوَّلاً إلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديق ولم يبيّن أنَّ في كلِّ منهما ضروريا ونظريا يمكن اكتسابُهُ مِنَ الضَّروريّ ؛ لجازَ أنْ يكونَ التَّصوراتُ بأثرها مثلاً ضروريةً ؛ فَلا حاجة إذاً إلى الموصل إلى التّصوُّرِ، فلا يثبتُ الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً، فإن قُلْتَ: يمكن أن يقسم العلمُ أوَّلاً إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ، ثمَّ يقسمه إلى التصورِ والتصديق، والجواب : أنَّ هذا الأسلوب مع كونِهِ مُوجباً لبتر نظم المقدّماتِ؛ قلب للمعقول ؛ لأنَّ التَّقسيم باعتبار كيفيَّةِ الحصولِ بعد التقسيم باعتبار الحصول نفْسِهِ؛ فإنَّ تقسيمَ العِلْم إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ ؛ تقسيم له باعتبار الكيفية معنى عارضِ لِكُلِّ مِنهُمَا ، والتَّقسيم باعتبار الحصولِ سابق في نظر العقل على التقسيم باعتبار الكيفية والصفة.
(قَوْلُهُ : شَرَعَ فِي التَّقْسِيم أي : تقسيم العِلْمِ أوَّلاً إلى التّصورِ والتَّصديقِ، ثمَّ تقسيم كلّ واحدٍ منهما إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظري، قال العماد في حواشِي الشَّمسيَّةِ: تقسيمُ العِلْمِ إلى التّصورِ والتصديقِ، مِن قبيل تقسيم الجنس، إلى الأنواعِ الَّتي يكونُ الامتياز الحاصِلُ منه امتيازاً ذاتيًا؛ بِخِلافِ القسمة إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ، فإِنَّ التَّمييزَ الحَاصِلَ منهُ تمييز عَرَضِيٌّ، وتقسيمُ الشَّيْء بِحسبِ الذَّاتِ مُقدَّم على التَّقسيم بحسب الوصف، والذي يدلُّ على ما ذكرنا مِن أَنَّ تقسيم الأَوَّلِ بحسبِ الذَّاتِ، والثَّاني بحسبِ الوصفِ ؛ عدم انقلابِ التَّصورِ تصديقاً؛ وبالعكس، وانقلاب النَّظري ضروريا ؛ وبالعكس.
Introduction of the author: The explanation of the need leading to the definition of logic. Dasuqi: ... [since] if knowledge were not first divided into them, and it were not clarified that in each there is the necessary and theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, then it would be possible that all concepts are necessary, so there would be no need for the discussions that lead to concept, and that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for the discussions that lead to assent, thus the need for both parts of logic would not be established. And you have known that what is meant by explanation of the need is what was mentioned, and by the mentioned division you find the intended purpose. And since the division into concept and assent is primary, and the division into necessary and theoretical is secondary, he gave precedence to the former over the latter. (His saying: 'al-munsāq') is an adjective for bayān, meaning the one leading to the definition of the science of logic by description; because the explanation of the need necessitates its definition by description, not by essential definition; because its benefit is the infallibility of thought from error, and this necessitates its definition, and it is a legal instrument that safeguards the mind from error in thought, as previously mentioned. Attar: (His saying: 'yunsāqu') means it entails, and he only used 'insiyāq' to indicate the obviousness of the implication, unlike if he had used 'yasūqu', because one might imagine effort. In choosing 'insiyāq' there is an indication that its entailment of it is not an introduction to the author's composition, as ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm informed. By that composition he means the saying of the author of al-Shamsiyya: 'Knowledge is either a concept or a concept accompanied by a judgment, up to his saying: so the need arose for a law that safeguards from it, and that is logic.' And the author abbreviated it here. So the ultimate point to which the explanation of the need leads is that the need arose for a law that provides infallibility of the mind from error in thought, and it is a necessary predicate meaning logic. Therefore the author said: 'and that is logic.' So it is established that the explanation of the need includes the definition of logic by its description. As for the definition, it is not entailed by the explanation of the need, because it is possible that the description is by something other than its purpose. It cannot be said: the explanation of the need is of the type of assent and the description is a concept; so how can one arrive at the concept through assent, while the reality is the opposite? The answer: the explanation of the need ends at the description and entails it, but it does not necessarily follow from that that it leads to it; this is entailment, not deduction. It is dependent on the division of knowledge into its two parts; he began the division and said: Dasuqi: (His saying: 'mawqūfan') meaning dependent. (His saying: 'he began the division') is an explicit statement in place of an implicit one. Attar: And (His saying: 'on the division of knowledge') — it cannot be said that the explanation of the need does not depend on all these premises; rather it is sufficient to say: knowledge is divided into necessary and theoretical... etc., what he mentioned. Because we say: the purpose is to explain the need for logic in both its parts. For if knowledge were not first divided into concept and assent, and it were not clarified that in each there is the necessary and theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, then it would be possible that all concepts are, for example, necessary, so there would be no need for the source leading to concept, so the need for both parts of logic together would not be established. If you say: it is possible to first divide knowledge into necessary and theoretical, then divide it into concept and assent. The answer: this method, besides being a cause for truncating the order of premises, is a reversal of what is reasonable. Because the division according to the mode of acquisition comes after the division according to acquisition itself. For the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical is a division of it according to the mode, an accidental meaning for each of them. And the division according to acquisition is prior in the mind's view to the division according to mode and attribute. (His saying: 'he began the division') means: the division of knowledge first into concept and assent, then the division of each of them into necessary and theoretical. Al-ʿImād said in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya: The division of knowledge into concept and assent is of the type of division of genus into species where the distinction resulting from it is an essential distinction, unlike the division into necessary and theoretical, for the distinction resulting from it is an accidental distinction. And the division of a thing according to essence is prior to the division according to attribute. What indicates what we mentioned that the first is according to essence and the second according to attribute is that concept does not change into assent and vice versa, while the theoretical changes into necessary and vice versa.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an introductory commentary on the matn's 'muqaddimat al-matin,' focusing on the explanation of the need (bayān al-ḥājah) for logic and the proper order of dividing knowledge. The commentators—Dasuqi and Attar—expand on the matn's claim that the explanation of the need leads to a definition of logic by description (rasm). They argue that if knowledge were not first divided into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), and then each into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), one might mistakenly think all concepts or assents are necessary, eliminating the need for logic's two parts (concept-acquisition and assent-acquisition). The priority of the division into concept/assent over necessary/theoretical is justified: the former is a division by essence (dhātī), while the latter is by accident (ʿaraḍī). Dasuqi quotes the matn's phrase 'al-munsāq' as leading to definition by rasm, explaining that bayān al-ḥājah entails the definition but does not produce it directly—it is an entailment, not a deduction. Attar then addresses a potential objection: how can bayān al-ḥājah, which is an assent (tasdiq), lead to a definition (a concept)? The answer is that it entails the definition without being the means of its acquisition. Finally, the commentators defend the necessity of the full set of premises, showing that a truncated division (e.g., only into necessary/theoretical) would fail to prove the need for both parts of logic. They conclude with al-ʿImād's observation that the essential division (concept/assent) is prior because its categories are mutually exclusive and non-convertible, unlike the accidental division (necessary/theoretical) where conversion is possible.

Key Terms
بيان الحاجة
رسم
منطق
انسياق
لزوم

A) Because the purpose of logic (preventing error) is its most salient feature and forms a functional definition.
B) Because the essence of logic is unknown and cannot be defined by hadd.
C) Because the matn explicitly says 'al-munsāq' implies a rasm.
D) Because all definitions in logic are by rasm.

Key Takeaway
The need for logic leads naturally to its definition by description (rasm) as an instrument that safeguards the mind from error, without requiring an essential definition.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
The Priority of Essential Division: Tasawwur/Tasdiq over Ḍarūrī/Naẓarī
Commentary on the proper order of dividing knowledge: essential before accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq must precede the division into daruri and nazari, using the distinction between essential and accidental division.

Core Idea
Essential division (by genus/species) is logically prior to accidental division (by attributes).

The commentators argue that knowledge must first be divided into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) before dividing into necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). This ordering is not arbitrary but reflects a logical priority: the first is a division by essence, the second by accident. Understanding this priority is crucial for grasping why the need for logic must be established for both parts simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of knowledge
The passage considers two ways to divide knowledge: (1) into tasawwur and tasdiq (concept and assent), and (2) into daruri and nazari (necessary and theoretical). The first is by the nature of knowledge itself, the second by how it is acquired.
Tasawwur is a mental representation without judgment; tasdiq is a judgment. Daruri is self-evident; nazari requires reasoning.
2
Understand why the first division is essential and the second accidental
Al-Imad is quoted: the division into tasawwur/tasdiq is like dividing genus into species—the distinction is essential (dhātī). In contrast, daruri/nazari is an accidental division (bi-ḥasab al-waṣf) because the same concept can be daruri to one person and nazari to another.
Essential divisions are mutually exclusive and exhaustive; accidental divisions can change.
3
See the logical priority of essential division over accidental
Just as you must know what a thing is before you can describe its attributes, you must first divide knowledge into its primary types (concept/assent) before considering how each type is acquired (necessary/theoretical). The commentators say this is 'prior in the mind's view.'
This prevents the error of thinking all knowledge is necessary, which would negate the need for logic.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
In fiqh, rulings are first divided into the five categories (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram)—an essential division based on the nature of the ruling. Then each can be divided into conditions that affect its validity (accidental). The essential division must come first to frame the discussion.
This parallel helps internalize the logical principle.

بيانُ الحاجة المنساقُ إلى تعريف المنطقِ
الدسوقي
تصديق ؛ إذْ لو لم يقسم العِلْم أوّلاً لهما، ولم يبيّن أنَّ في كلِّ منهما ضَروريًا ونظريا يمكن اكتسابُهُ من الضَّروريّ؛ لجَازَ أنْ تكونَ التَّصوّرات كلّها ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجةً إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصوُّرِ ، وأن تكونَ التَّصديقات كلُّهَا ضروريَّةٌ، فَلا حاجة إذاً إلى مباحث الموصل للتَّصديق، فلم يثبت الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق، وقد علمتَ أنَّ المراد ببيان الحاجة ما ذكر، وبالتقسيم المذكور تجد المقصود المذكور، وَلَمَّا كانَ التَّقسيم إلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ أوَّليًّا، والتَّقسيم إلى الضروري والنظري ثانويا ؛ قدَّمَ ذاكَ على هذا . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُنْسَاقُ) صفةٌ لبيان ؛ أي: المؤدّي إلى تعريف عِلْم المنطقِ بالرَّسمِ؛ لأنَّ بيان الحاجةِ يستلزم تعريفَهُ بالرَّسم لا بالحدِّ؛ لأنَّ فائدتَهُ عصمتُه الفكر عن الخطأ، وهذا يستلزم تعريفه، وهو آلة قانونية تعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكرِ كَمَا سبق .

(قَوْلُهُ: يَنْسَاقُ) أي : يستلزم، وإنَّما عبر بالانسياق؛ إشارة إلى ظهورِ اللُّزومِ، بِخلافِ مَا لو عبَّرَ بِيسوقُ ؛ فربَّما يتوهَّمُ المعاناة، ففي اختيار الانسياق إشارة إلى أَنَّ استلزامَهُ إِيَّاهُ غيرُ مدخل لتحرير المصنفِ ، أفاده عبد الحكيم، ويعني بذلك التحرير قول صاحبِ الشَّمسيَّةِ : العِلْمُ إِمَّا تَصوُّرٌ، وإِمَّا تَصورٌ معه حكم، إلى قوله : فمسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يعصم عنه، وهو المنطقُ ، وقد اختصرها المصنِّفُ هنا ؛ فَآخِرُ مَا ينساقُ إليه بيان الحاجة ؛ أَنَّهُ مَسَّتِ الحاجة إلى قانون يفيد عصمة الذهنِ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وهو لازِم محمولٌ مُساءٍ للمنطق، ولذا ؛ قال المصنِّفُ : وهو المنطقُ . فثبتَ أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ مُتضمِّنُ لتعريف المنطقِ برسمِهِ، وأمَّا التَّعريفُ ؛ فلا يستلزم ببيان الحاجة؛ لجواز أن يكونَ الرَّسم بشيءٍ آخر دونَ غايتِهِ، لا يُقالُ: إِنَّ بيان الحاجة مِن قبيل التّصديقِ والرَّسم تصوُّرُ ؛ فكيف يتوصَّلُ للتَّصورِ بالتصديقِ، مع أنَّ الواقع العكس؟ والجواب : أنَّ بيان الحاجةِ ينتهي إلى الرسم ويستلزمه، ولا يلزمُ من ذلك أن يكونَ مُوصلاً له، فهذا استلزام لا استنتاج
موقوفاً على تقسيم العِلْمِ إلى قسميهِ؛ شَرَعَ في التقسيم فقال :

(قَوْلُهُ : شرع في التقسيم) إظهار في محلّ الإضمار .
العطار
و
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى تَقْسِيم (الْعِلْم لا يُقال: إن بيان الحاجة لا يتوقف على جميع هذه المقدِّماتِ، بل يكفي أنْ يُقَالَ: العلم ينقسم إلى ضروري ونظري ... إلخ، ما ذكرَهُ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : المقصود بيان الاحتياج إلى المنطق بقسميه، فلو لم يقسم العلم أوَّلاً إلى التَّصورِ والتَّصديق ولم يبيّن أنَّ في كلِّ منهما ضروريا ونظريا يمكن اكتسابُهُ مِنَ الضَّروريّ ؛ لجازَ أنْ يكونَ التَّصوراتُ بأثرها مثلاً ضروريةً ؛ فَلا حاجة إذاً إلى الموصل إلى التّصوُّرِ، فلا يثبتُ الاحتياج إلى جزأي المنطق معاً، فإن قُلْتَ: يمكن أن يقسم العلمُ أوَّلاً إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ، ثمَّ يقسمه إلى التصورِ والتصديق، والجواب : أنَّ هذا الأسلوب مع كونِهِ مُوجباً لبتر نظم المقدّماتِ؛ قلب للمعقول ؛ لأنَّ التَّقسيم باعتبار كيفيَّةِ الحصولِ بعد التقسيم باعتبار الحصول نفْسِهِ؛ فإنَّ تقسيمَ العِلْم إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ ؛ تقسيم له باعتبار الكيفية معنى عارضِ لِكُلِّ مِنهُمَا ، والتَّقسيم باعتبار الحصولِ سابق في نظر العقل على التقسيم باعتبار الكيفية والصفة.
(قَوْلُهُ : شَرَعَ فِي التَّقْسِيم أي : تقسيم العِلْمِ أوَّلاً إلى التّصورِ والتَّصديقِ، ثمَّ تقسيم كلّ واحدٍ منهما إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظري، قال العماد في حواشِي الشَّمسيَّةِ: تقسيمُ العِلْمِ إلى التّصورِ والتصديقِ، مِن قبيل تقسيم الجنس، إلى الأنواعِ الَّتي يكونُ الامتياز الحاصِلُ منه امتيازاً ذاتيًا؛ بِخِلافِ القسمة إلى الضَّروريّ والنَّظريّ، فإِنَّ التَّمييزَ الحَاصِلَ منهُ تمييز عَرَضِيٌّ، وتقسيمُ الشَّيْء بِحسبِ الذَّاتِ مُقدَّم على التَّقسيم بحسب الوصف، والذي يدلُّ على ما ذكرنا مِن أَنَّ تقسيم الأَوَّلِ بحسبِ الذَّاتِ، والثَّاني بحسبِ الوصفِ ؛ عدم انقلابِ التَّصورِ تصديقاً؛ وبالعكس، وانقلاب النَّظري ضروريا ؛ وبالعكس.
Introduction of the author: The explanation of the need leading to the definition of logic. Dasuqi: ... [since] if knowledge were not first divided into them, and it were not clarified that in each there is the necessary and theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, then it would be possible that all concepts are necessary, so there would be no need for the discussions that lead to concept, and that all assents are necessary, so there would be no need for the discussions that lead to assent, thus the need for both parts of logic would not be established. And you have known that what is meant by explanation of the need is what was mentioned, and by the mentioned division you find the intended purpose. And since the division into concept and assent is primary, and the division into necessary and theoretical is secondary, he gave precedence to the former over the latter. (His saying: 'al-munsāq') is an adjective for bayān, meaning the one leading to the definition of the science of logic by description; because the explanation of the need necessitates its definition by description, not by essential definition; because its benefit is the infallibility of thought from error, and this necessitates its definition, and it is a legal instrument that safeguards the mind from error in thought, as previously mentioned. Attar: (His saying: 'yunsāqu') means it entails, and he only used 'insiyāq' to indicate the obviousness of the implication, unlike if he had used 'yasūqu', because one might imagine effort. In choosing 'insiyāq' there is an indication that its entailment of it is not an introduction to the author's composition, as ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm informed. By that composition he means the saying of the author of al-Shamsiyya: 'Knowledge is either a concept or a concept accompanied by a judgment, up to his saying: so the need arose for a law that safeguards from it, and that is logic.' And the author abbreviated it here. So the ultimate point to which the explanation of the need leads is that the need arose for a law that provides infallibility of the mind from error in thought, and it is a necessary predicate meaning logic. Therefore the author said: 'and that is logic.' So it is established that the explanation of the need includes the definition of logic by its description. As for the definition, it is not entailed by the explanation of the need, because it is possible that the description is by something other than its purpose. It cannot be said: the explanation of the need is of the type of assent and the description is a concept; so how can one arrive at the concept through assent, while the reality is the opposite? The answer: the explanation of the need ends at the description and entails it, but it does not necessarily follow from that that it leads to it; this is entailment, not deduction. It is dependent on the division of knowledge into its two parts; he began the division and said: Dasuqi: (His saying: 'mawqūfan') meaning dependent. (His saying: 'he began the division') is an explicit statement in place of an implicit one. Attar: And (His saying: 'on the division of knowledge') — it cannot be said that the explanation of the need does not depend on all these premises; rather it is sufficient to say: knowledge is divided into necessary and theoretical... etc., what he mentioned. Because we say: the purpose is to explain the need for logic in both its parts. For if knowledge were not first divided into concept and assent, and it were not clarified that in each there is the necessary and theoretical that can be acquired from the necessary, then it would be possible that all concepts are, for example, necessary, so there would be no need for the source leading to concept, so the need for both parts of logic together would not be established. If you say: it is possible to first divide knowledge into necessary and theoretical, then divide it into concept and assent. The answer: this method, besides being a cause for truncating the order of premises, is a reversal of what is reasonable. Because the division according to the mode of acquisition comes after the division according to acquisition itself. For the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical is a division of it according to the mode, an accidental meaning for each of them. And the division according to acquisition is prior in the mind's view to the division according to mode and attribute. (His saying: 'he began the division') means: the division of knowledge first into concept and assent, then the division of each of them into necessary and theoretical. Al-ʿImād said in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya: The division of knowledge into concept and assent is of the type of division of genus into species where the distinction resulting from it is an essential distinction, unlike the division into necessary and theoretical, for the distinction resulting from it is an accidental distinction. And the division of a thing according to essence is prior to the division according to attribute. What indicates what we mentioned that the first is according to essence and the second according to attribute is that concept does not change into assent and vice versa, while the theoretical changes into necessary and vice versa.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an introductory commentary on the matn's 'muqaddimat al-matin,' focusing on the explanation of the need (bayān al-ḥājah) for logic and the proper order of dividing knowledge. The commentators—Dasuqi and Attar—expand on the matn's claim that the explanation of the need leads to a definition of logic by description (rasm). They argue that if knowledge were not first divided into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), and then each into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), one might mistakenly think all concepts or assents are necessary, eliminating the need for logic's two parts (concept-acquisition and assent-acquisition). The priority of the division into concept/assent over necessary/theoretical is justified: the former is a division by essence (dhātī), while the latter is by accident (ʿaraḍī). Dasuqi quotes the matn's phrase 'al-munsāq' as leading to definition by rasm, explaining that bayān al-ḥājah entails the definition but does not produce it directly—it is an entailment, not a deduction. Attar then addresses a potential objection: how can bayān al-ḥājah, which is an assent (tasdiq), lead to a definition (a concept)? The answer is that it entails the definition without being the means of its acquisition. Finally, the commentators defend the necessity of the full set of premises, showing that a truncated division (e.g., only into necessary/theoretical) would fail to prove the need for both parts of logic. They conclude with al-ʿImād's observation that the essential division (concept/assent) is prior because its categories are mutually exclusive and non-convertible, unlike the accidental division (necessary/theoretical) where conversion is possible.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Why 'Not Always Correct' Means Some Thought Can Be Correct
Author explains the logical form of 'not always correct'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'negation of generality' (salb al-‘umūm) and 'generality of negation' (‘umūm al-salb), and explain how this distinction justifies the need for a criterion (logic) in thought.

Core Idea
The statement “thought is not always correct” does not mean all thought is wrong; it means some thought can be correct.

The author of our text opens by saying that thought is not always correct. A superficial reading might conclude that thought is generally unreliable. But the commentator carefully unpacks the logical structure of this statement, showing that it is a particular negative (some thought is not correct) rather than a universal negative (no thought is correct). This subtle but crucial distinction reveals that some thought can be correct – and that is precisely why we need a criterion (logic) to distinguish correct thought from error. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify these two types of negation and see their practical importance in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The key phrase is 'ليس بصواب دائماً' (not always correct). Notice that the word 'دائماً' (always) attaches to the negated, not the negation.
2
Understand the two possible interpretations
Either it means 'thought is never correct' (universal negative) or 'some thought is not correct' (particular negative). The commentator argues the latter is intended.
3
Learn the logical terminology
'سلب العموم' (negation of generality) = particular negative; 'عموم السلب' (generality of negation) = universal negative. This distinction is standard in Islamic logic.
4
Apply to an example
In 'I did not master all the sciences' (particular negative), the negation is not universal; some sciences were mastered. Similarly, 'thought is not always correct' leaves room for correct thought.
5
Connect to the purpose of logic
If all thought were incorrect, logic would be useless. But since some thought is correct, we need a criterion (logic) to identify which thought is correct and which is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الْفِكْرَ) أي: الَّذي هو النَّظرُ المكتسب به؛ لأنَّه يكون به اکتساب العلوم النَّظريَّةِ ؛ تصوريَّةً كانت أو تصديقية، وقوله: (ليس بصواب)؛ أي: بمصيب دائماً؛ أي: في كلِّ الأوقات، وهذا قيد في المنفي لا في النَّفي، وإلا ; لاقتضى أنَّ عدمَ الصَّوابِ دائم، مع أنه ليس بمراد . والحاصلُ : أنَّ قولَه : (ليس بصواب دائماً ) من باب سلب العموم ، وحينئذ ; فيصدقُ بصورتين؛ إحداهما : أنْ لا يكون فرد من أفراد الفكر صواباً، والآخر: أن يكونَ بعض أفرادِهِ ليس بصواب، وبعضُهُ الآخَرُ صواباً ، وهذه الصورة هي المرادةُ ; لأنَّها المحققة. (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّ الْفِكْرَ) أي : الَّذي هو النَّظَرُ المكتسب به، فيكون الاكتساب كذلك محشّي، أراد بالمكتسب به ما يقعُ بِهِ الاكتساب ، وهو نَفْسُ النَّظَرِ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قالَ: النَّظرُ الكاسبُ ، وأمَّا قولُه : فيكون الاكتساب كذلك ; إنْ أرادَ بِهِ المعنى المصدري ; أعني : تحصيل الطَّريقِ الكاسب ; فهذا أمر اعتباريٌّ ; لأنَّ المعاني المصدريَّةً أمورٌ نسبية اعتباريَّةٌ، فَلَا تُوصَفُ بخطأ ولا صواب، وإن أراد الاكتساب بمعنى اسم المفعول; ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : سلب العموم) الفرق بينه وبين عموم السَّلب أن الأوّل: موجة فيه السبب لبعض الأفراد كما هنا وكقولك : لم أتقن كلَّ العلوم، فهو في قوة السالبة الجزئية أي : بعض الفكر ليس بصواب وبعض العلوم لم أتقنها، والثَّاني : هو عموم السَّلب قد يوجه فيه السَّلب إلى كلّ الأفراد نحو كل حيوان لم يخلق عبثاً فهو في قوَّة السَّالبة الكلية : أي لا شيء من الحيوان بمخلوق عبثاً . ا . هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
The author's introduction. His saying: 'because thought' — i.e., which is the consideration (naẓar) by which acquisition (iktisāb) occurs; because through it theoretical sciences are acquired, whether conceptual or assertive. And his saying: 'is not correct' — i.e., not hitting the mark always; i.e., at all times. This is a restriction on the negated, not on the negation; otherwise it would imply that incorrectness is perpetual, which is not intended. The upshot: his saying 'is not always correct' falls under negation of generality (salb al-‘umūm). It applies in two forms: one, that no individual of thought is correct; the other, that some individuals of thought are not correct and the rest are correct. This latter form is intended because it is realized. His saying: 'because thought' — i.e., which is the consideration by which acquisition occurs, so acquisition is likewise glossed. He meant by 'by which acquisition occurs' that which acquisition occurs through, which is consideration itself, as if he said: the acquiring consideration. As for his saying: 'so acquisition is likewise' — if he means the verbal noun meaning, i.e., obtaining the acquiring path, then this is a relative matter, for verbal noun meanings are relative matters and are not described as error or correctness. If he means acquisition in the sense of the passive participle, end quote. His saying: 'negation of generality' — the difference between it and generality of negation is that in the former, the negation is directed to some individuals, as here and as in your saying: 'I did not master all the sciences.' It is equivalent to a particular negative, i.e., some thought is not correct and some sciences I did not master. The second is generality of negation, where the negation is directed to all individuals, as in 'every animal was not created in vain.' It is equivalent to a universal negative, i.e., no animal is created in vain. End quote. Al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's (al-mātin) introductory statement: 'because thought is not always correct.' The commentator, likely al-Dasūqī, begins by defining 'thought' (al-fikr) as 'consideration' (al-naẓar) by which acquisition (iktisāb) of theoretical sciences occurs, both conceptual and assertive. Then he clarifies the phrase 'is not always correct': it means 'not hitting the mark at all times.' He emphasizes that the word 'always' (dā’iman) is a restriction on the negated (al-manfī), not on the negation (al-nafy). If it were on the negation, it would mean that thought is perpetually incorrect, which is not intended. Rather, it is an instance of 'negation of generality' (salb al-‘umūm), which is logically equivalent to a particular negative (al-sālibah al-juz’iyyah), i.e., some thought is not correct, while the rest is correct. This justifies the need for a criterion (logic) to distinguish correct thought from error. The commentator then revisits the definition, stressing that thought is the consideration that acquires, and acquisition is itself a relative verbal noun that does not admit truth or falsity. Finally, a marginal note by al-Sharnūbī distinguishes between 'negation of generality' (salb al-‘umūm) and 'generality of negation' (‘umūm al-salb). The first is a particular negative, as in 'I did not master all the sciences,' meaning some sciences were not mastered. The second is a universal negative, as in 'no animal was created in vain.' This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting the author's statement as asserting that some thought is correct and some is not, not that all thought is incorrect.

Key Terms
الفكر
سلب العموم
عموم السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, the statement 'thought is not always correct' is an example of which logical form?
A) Universal negative (no thought is correct).
B) Particular negative (some thought is not correct).
C) Universal affirmative (all thought is correct).
D) Particular affirmative (some thought is correct).

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'thought is not always correct' is a particular negative, meaning some thought is correct, which justifies the need for logic as a criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Qur'anic verse: 'Allah does not wrong anyone at all' (Qur'an 4:40). Is this an example of salb al-‘umūm or ‘umūm al-salb? Explain why.
?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
The Argument from Contradiction: Why Thought Is Not Always Correct
Dassuqi and Attar explain how contradictions prove thought is fallible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how contradiction among rational people and self-contradiction demonstrate that human thought is fallible, and distinguish between the probabilistic and certain forms of the argument.

Core Idea
Contradiction proves fallacy: not all thought is correct.

This passage uses a simple but powerful observation: rational people disagree, and sometimes even a single person contradicts himself. The logicians argue that this shows human thought (fikr) does not always lead to truth. We will unpack the reasoning and see why self-contradiction gives certain knowledge while external disagreement only gives probability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of contradiction
The matn states that rational people contradict each other, and even a single person contradicts himself. Look for the Arabic words 'عقلاء' and 'الإنسان الواحد'.
2
Analyze the logical structure of external contradiction
Dassuqi gives the example of the Sunni who assents to the temporal origination of the world and the philosopher who assents to its pre-eternity. Since these are contradictory, they cannot both be true and cannot both be false, so at least one is false. Therefore, not every thought is correct.
This is an immediate inference from the law of non-contradiction.
3
Contrast with self-contradiction
Attar explains that self-contradiction yields certainty (jazm) because we know our own contradictory beliefs directly, whereas external contradiction only yields probability (ẓann) because we infer others' beliefs from their statements.
The term 'جزم' is key here.
4
Address the potential objection
Attar responds to the objection that rational people might err because they are not seeking truth but engaging in skepticism. He argues that the term 'rational people' (عقلاء) inherently implies seeking truth, so the argument stands.
5
Extract the epistemological lesson
The passage teaches that thought is not an infallible source of knowledge. This motivates the need for logical tools to assess arguments.
This sets up the whole science of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُناقِضُ العقلاء بعضُهم بعضاً ؟! ، بل الإنسانُ الواحد يناقض نفْسَه
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يَنَاقَضُ الْعُقَلَاءُ بَعْضُهُمْ بَعْضَاً أي : في مُقتضى أفكارهم ؛ فَبعضُهُم كالسُّنِّي أَذَاهُ فكرُهُ إلى التصديقِ بحدوثِ العالَم، وبعضُهُم كَالفلسفيّ أَذَاهُ فكرُهُ إلى التصديقِ بقِدَم العَالَم، وحينئذ ؛ فأحد الفكرين غيرُ صواب؛ لأنَّه لا يمكنُ أنْ يكونَ كلا الفكرَيْنِ صَواباً ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ عليهِ من اجتماع النقيضين، وَلَا خطأً؛ لِمَا يلزم عليه من ارتفاعِ النَّقيضَيْنِ، وهو مُحال، فتعيَّن أن يكون أحدهما صواباً والآخرُ خطأ، وحينئذ ؛ فلا يكونُ الفكرُ صواباً دائماً .
(قَوْلُهُ : بَلِ الْإِنْسَانُ الْوَاحِدُ ... إلخ) إضراب انتقالي أتى به ؛ لأَنَّهُ أَظهرُ مِمَّا قبلَهُ فِي إفادةِ أنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً ؛ لأنَّ مُناقضة العقلاء بعضهم بعضاً إِنَّما تفيدُ الظَّنَّ بأَنَّ الفكر ليس بصوابِ دائماً ، بخلافِ مُناقضة العاقل نفسه ؛ فإنَّها تفيد الجزم بذلك، فتكون دلالتها أقوى وأظهرَ مِن دلالةِ مُناقضة العقلاء، وذلكَ لأَنَّ مُناقضةً بعض العقلاء بعضاً ؛ إِنَّما تُعلمُ مِن عباراتِهم الدَّالَّة على أنَّ مُقتضيات أفكارهم متناقضة.
(قَوْلُهُ: يُنَاقِضُ الْعُقَلَاءِ بَعْضُهُمْ بَعْضَاً لا يُقالُ : يجوز أن يكونَ الخطأُ لعدم طلبهم الصَّواب، بل مُجرَّدُ التَّشكيكِ كما يعرضُ في بعض أحوال المناظِرَةِ، ولذلكَ وقع في عبارة غيره توصيفهم بالطَّالبين للصَّوابِ لِدَفع هذهِ الصَّورة؛ لأنَّا نقولُ: استغنى الشَّارِحُ عن هذا الوصف لإشعارِ لفظ العقلاء بِهِ؛ إذْ شأنُ العاقِل طَلَبُ الصَّوابِ لا التَّشكيكُ والتَّغليظُ، نَعَمْ؛ قد يضطر في بعض الأحوالِ لِذلكَ كَمَا قِيلَ: لَئِنْ كنتُ مُحتاجاً إلى العِلْمِ إِنَّني إلى الجهل في بعض المواضع أحوج لأنَّ هذهِ حالةُ ضرورة لا تُعتبر .
(قَوْلُهُ : بَلِ الْإِنْسَانُ) إضرابٌ عَن قولِهِ : (وقدْ يُناقض العقلاء... إلخ) وهو للتّرقّي، فإِنَّ هَذهِ الحالة أظهرُ ؛ لأنَّ اطَّلاعَ الشَّخص على حالِ نَفْسِهِ أظهرُ مِن اطلاعِهِ على حالِ غيرِهِ، ثمَّ إِنَّ الشَّارِحَ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تعالى؛ أخذ البيانَ عاماً شاملاً
The text of the author: 'And indeed rational people contradict one another? Nay, the single person contradicts himself.' (Commentary by Dassuqi: 'His statement: “And indeed rational people contradict one another” means in the implications of their thoughts. Some of them, like the Sunni, his thought leads him to assent to the temporal origination of the world; and some of them, like the philosopher, his thought leads him to assent to the pre-eternity of the world. Then, one of the two thoughts is not correct because it is impossible for both thoughts to be correct due to the necessary consequence of the conjunction of two contradictories, nor both incorrect due to the necessary consequence of the removal of the two contradictories, which is impossible. So it is determined that one of them is correct and the other is incorrect. Then, thought is not always correct. (His statement: “Nay, the single person...” etc.) is a transitional digression that he brought because it is more evident than what precedes it in conveying that thought is not always correct. For the contradiction of rational people with each other only yields probability that thought is not always correct, unlike the contradiction of the rational person with himself, for it yields certainty of that. So its indication is stronger and more evident than the indication of contradiction among rational people, because the contradiction of some rational people with others is only known from their statements that indicate that the implications of their thoughts are contradictory. (Commentary by Attar: 'His statement: “Rational people contradict one another” — it cannot be said: it is possible that the error is due to their not seeking correctness, but mere skepticism as occurs in some states of disputation, and therefore in the expression of someone else they are described as 'seekers of correctness' to repudiate this situation. For we say: the commentator dispensed with this description because the word 'rational people' indicates it, for the affair of the rational person is seeking correctness, not skepticism and deception. Yes, he may be forced in some situations to that, as it is said: 'If I am in need of knowledge, I am in more need of ignorance in some places' because this is a situation of necessity that is not considered. (His statement: “Nay, the person” is a digression from his statement 'And indeed rational people contradict...' and it is for progression, for this state is more evident because the person's knowledge of his own state is more evident than his knowledge of the state of another. Then the commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, took the explanation as general and comprehensive.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from contradiction to establish that human thought is not infallible. The author begins with the matn stating that rational people contradict each other, and even a single person contradicts himself. Dassuqi explains the first part: rational people like the Sunni and the philosopher arrive at contradictory assents (taṣdīq) regarding the world's origin. Since both cannot be true (contradictories cannot coexist) and cannot both be false (contradictories cannot be removed simultaneously), one must be true and the other false. Therefore, not every thought (fikr) is correct. He then explains the second part: self-contradiction is a stronger proof because while external contradiction yields only probability (ẓann), one's own internal contradiction yields certainty (jazm). Attar addresses an objection: perhaps the rational people's error is due to not seeking truth but merely engaging in skepticism. He argues that the term 'rational people' inherently implies seeking truth, so no additional qualifier is needed. He also notes that the digression to self-contradiction is for progression (tarqī), as self-knowledge is more evident than knowledge of others. The commentator concludes that the explanation is general and comprehensive.

Key Terms
عقلاء
تصديق
نقيضان
فكر
جزم

Checkpoint
Why does Dassuqi say that self-contradiction is a stronger proof than contradiction among different rational people?
A) Because self-contradiction involves more people.
B) Because one is certain of one's own beliefs, whereas others' beliefs are only known through statements.
C) Because self-contradiction is rarer.
D) Because self-contradiction always involves necessity.

Key Takeaway
Human thought is not infallible; contradictions among rational people and within a single person show that some thoughts are in error, necessitating criteria for sound reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disagreement between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya on the issue of free will (al-qadr). Explain how the argument from contradiction would apply to their contradictory assents. Which assent is more likely to be incorrect? Does this prove that both are incorrect? (Hint: Use the logical principle that contradictory propositions cannot both be false.)
?
Identify a case in your own experience where you held contradictory beliefs. How does self-contradiction provide certainty that at least one of your beliefs is false? Write a brief analysis.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Need for a Lawful Discipline to Protect from Error
Dassuqi and Attar explain the necessity of logic as a discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is necessary and why it must terminate in necessary knowledge, and distinguish the position of al-Razi from that of Dassuqi regarding the scope of logic.

Core Idea
Logic is the law that protects the mind from error by providing methods to derive theoretical knowledge from necessary starting points.

This passage from the commentary on the Tadhheeb establishes the very foundation of logic: why it is needed and what it does. You already know that human beings can err in their reasoning. Logic is presented as a "law" (qānūn) that safeguards the mind from such error. The commentator Dassuqi explains that logic provides the methods (turūq) — both for proofs (assents) and for explaining statements (concepts) — and that these methods must ultimately lead back to necessary (self-evident) knowledge to avoid circularity or infinite regression. Attar then discusses an important nuance: does logic cover both concepts and assents, or only assents? Al-Razi limited it to assents, but the commentator Dassuqi includes both, and Attar applauds this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: Why do we need logic?
The matn starts with 'So we need a law that protects from error...' Recognize that the necessity arises from human fallibility in reasoning.
2
Understand the definition of logic
Logic is a law (qānūn) that provides methods for acquiring theoretical knowledge (naẓariyyāt) from necessary knowledge (ḍarūriyyāt). Note that theoretical knowledge requires reasoning, while necessary knowledge is self-evident.
The methods include proofs (ḥujaj) and explaining statements (qawl shāriḥ), covering both assents and concepts.
3
Trace the chain of reasoning
The acquisition must end at necessary knowledge to prevent circularity (dawr) or infinite regression (tasalsul). Even if theoretical knowledge is derived from other theoretical knowledge, the chain must ultimately reach a necessary premise.
4
Compare the scope debate
Al-Razi limited logic to assents (taṣdīqāt) only, arguing that concepts (taṣawwurāt) are not subject to error themselves. But Attar notes that error can arise from implicit judgments attached to concepts, so including both is more beneficial. Recognize that Dassuqi's broader definition is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتجنا إلى قانون عاصم عن الخطأ، مفيد لطُرُقِ اكتسابِ النَّظريَّاتِ من الضَّروريَّاتِ ، وذلك القانون هو : المنطقُ .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَاحْتَجْنَا إِلَى قَانُونٍ ... إلخ) هذا هو معنى قول المصنف الآتي: فاحتيج . . . إلخ ، وإِنَّما أتى بِهِ هُنا ؛ لأجل قوله : والحاصل ... إلخ، وإنَّما أتى بهذا الحاصل ؛ إشارةً لربط كلام المتن بعضه ببعض .
(قَوْلُهُ : إِلَى قَانُونِ) أي: ذي قانون، أو المراد به العِلْمُ نَفْسُه (قَوْلُهُ: مُفِيدٍ لِطُرُق) وهي الحجج وشرائطها، والقول الشارح وشرائطه؛ أي: طرقُ التصديقات والتصوراتِ النَّظرية .
(قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الضَّرُوْرِيَّاتِ مُتعلّق باكتساب، بمعنى تحصيل، وقوله: الضَّروريَّات ; أي : وَلَوْ بحسب آلاتِها، وحينئذ فيصدقُ باكتساب النَّظري من نظري آخر ، والنَّظريّ الآخَرِ من نظري ثالث، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهي إلى ضروريّ، فلا بُدَّ من الانتهاء للضَّروريّ ; دفعاً للدور أو التسلسل.
للتَّصوراتِ والتَّصديقات، والرَّازِيُّ في شَرْح الشَّمسيَّةِ خَصَّصَهُ بِحالِ التَّصديقاتِ ; حيثُ قالَ : فمن واحدٍ يتأدَّى فكرُهُ إلى التصديقِ بحدوثِ العالم ... إلخ، واعتذرُوا عنه بأنَّهُ لم يتعرّض للتَّصوراتِ ; لِعَدَمِ ظهور الخطأ فيها، فإِنَّ كُلَّ تَصوُّرٍ معنِّى مِنَ المعاني; لا تناقض ولا تمانُعَ بينَها ، إنَّما السَّمانُعُ بين الأحكام الضمنية اللازمة لها، وكذلكَ الكَسْبُ فيها غيرُ ظَاهِرٍ بناءً على شبهة الإمام المدعي ضَروريَّتَها كلَّها، وما صنعَهُ شارحُنَا أَفْيَدُ وأَحْسَنُ .
So we need a law that protects from error, beneficial for the methods of acquiring theoretical things from necessary things, and that law is logic. (His statement: 'So we need a law...' etc.) This is the meaning of the later statement of the author: 'So we need...' etc. He only brought it here for the sake of his statement: 'And the conclusion...' etc. He brought this conclusion as an indication of linking the text's words together. (His statement: 'to a law') i.e., a thing possessing law, or what is meant is the science itself. (His statement: 'beneficial for methods') which are the proofs and their conditions, and the explaining statement and its conditions; that is, the methods of theoretical assents and concepts. (His statement: 'from necessary things' is attached to 'acquisition', meaning attainment. His statement: 'the necessary things' means even if according to their instruments. Then it applies to acquiring the theoretical from another theoretical, and that other theoretical from a third theoretical, and so on until it ends at a necessary, so there must be an ending at the necessary, to repudiate circularity or regression. For concepts and assents, and al-Razi in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya specified it for the case of assents, for he said: 'For one person his thought leads him to assent to the temporal origination of the world...' etc. They excused him because he did not address concepts due to the lack of apparent error in them, for every concept of the meanings does not involve contradiction or mutual prevention; rather, mutual prevention occurs among the implicit judgments necessary for them. Likewise, acquisition in them is not apparent based on the Imam's doubt claiming all of them are necessary. What our commentator has done is more beneficial and better.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the purpose and scope of logic. The matn states that we need a 'law' (qānūn) that protects from error, providing methods to acquire theoretical knowledge (naẓariyyāt) from necessary knowledge (ḍarūriyyāt), and that law is logic. Dassuqi explains that this law corresponds to the author's later saying, and that it is brought here to link the text. He clarifies that 'law' can mean a discipline or the science itself. The 'methods' include proofs (ḥujaj) and their conditions, as well as the explaining statement (qawl shāriḥ) and its conditions—that is, the methods for theoretical assents (taṣdīqāt) and concepts (taṣawwurāt). The acquisition must eventually terminate at necessary knowledge to avoid infinite regress or circularity. Attar then discusses an issue: al-Razi in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya limited the scope to assents, arguing that error is not apparent in concepts because concepts themselves do not contradict each other; contradiction arises from implicit judgments attached to them. However, Attar praises his own commentator (Dassuqi) for including both concepts and assents, deeming it more beneficial and better.

Key Terms
قانون
منطق
ضروريات
نظريات
تصورات
تصديقات

D) Because logic is only about certainty, not probability.

Key Takeaway
Logic is the essential discipline that provides the rules to safeguard reasoning from error, and it covers both concepts and assents because error can infiltrate through implicit judgments attached to concepts.

The Purpose of Logic: Safeguarding the Mind from Error
Clarifying the need for logic as safeguard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose of logic as described by Dasuqi and Attar and explain why it is needed.

Core Idea
Logic is the safeguard for the mind against error in thought.

This passage expands on the matn's brief statement about the need for logic. The commentators address the question: 'In what do people need logic?' and answer that it is for safeguarding the mind from error. This establishes the foundational purpose of logic as a protective tool for correct reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Find where al-Dasuqi states the question being answered: 'in any matter do people need logic?'
The question is implied from the earlier discussion about the fallibility of thought.
2
Extract the answer
The answer is the safeguard (isma) of the mind from error in thought. Logic is needed for this purpose.
Al-Dasuqi explicitly says the need is for the mentioned safeguard.
3
Understand the grammatical parsing
Al-‘Attar analyzes the sentence structure: 'that people...' is a source clause acting as deputy subject, and 'in any matter' is fronted due to the interrogative 'any'.
This grammatical clarification supports the logical point.
4
Connect to the goal of the science
The safeguard (isma) is identified as the ultimate benefit (ghaya) and purpose of the science of logic.
This is reinforced by the phrase 'affirmation that it is the goal of this science and its benefit'.

Source Text

فعُلِمَ مِنْ هذا أنَّ النَّاسَ في أيّ شيءٍ يحتاجون إلى المنطق، وذلكَ بيانُ الحاجة
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ هَذَا ) أي : من هذا التَّقريرِ، وهو قوله : لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً، فاحتيج ... إلخ، كذا قرَّر بعضُهم، ولكنَّ الأوفق بقولِ الشَّارحِ سابقاً : ولمَّا كانَ بيانُ الحاجة المنساقُ لتعريف المنطقِ ... إلخ ، أنْ يُقالَ: فَعْلِمَ مِنْ هذا ؛ أي: مجموع قول المصنف : العِلْمِ إنْ كانَ إذعاناً للنّسبةِ، إلى قوله : وقد يقع فيه الخطأ، مع قول الشارح: فَاحتجنا لقانون ... إلخ. (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ النَّاسَ ) أي : جواب «أنَّ» ... إلخ، وهو عصمة الذهنِ عن الخطأ في الفكرِ ؛ أي : عُلِمَ مِمَّا سبق جوابُ هذا السُّؤالِ المصوَّرِ بقولِنَا : في أي شيء يحتاج النَّاسُ إلى المنطق؟ وجوابه : يحتاجون إليه في العصمة المذكورة، وقوله: (في أيّ شيءٍ مُتعلّق بيحتاجون، وقدَّم عليه ؛ لأنَّ أيَّا استفهاميّة، فَلَهَا الصدارة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَذَلِكَ بَيَانُ الْحَاجَةِ) أي : وذكرُ ذلكَ تبيين، أو: وذلك ذو بيانٍ للعصمة المذكورة ؛ أي: التصديقُ بأنَّها غاية هذا العِلْمِ، وفائدته .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ النَّاسَ ... إلخ) المصدرُ المنسبِكُ مِن (أَنَّ) معَ صِلَتِها من مادَّةِ خبرها، وهو يحتاجون، وهو نائب فاعل عُلِمَ بحذف مُضَاف، والتَّقديرُ : عُلِمَ جوابُ احتياج النَّاسِ ؛ أي: جوابُ السُّؤال عن ذلكَ، فإذا قيل : في أي شيء قِيْلَ يحتاجُ النَّاسُ إِلى المنطق؟ يُقَالُ في الجواب: لعصمةِ الفكر عن الخطأ، فـ «أيُّ» ههنا استفهاميَّةٌ ، وهي ومجرورُهَا مُتعلّقان بيحتاجونَ قُدِّما للصَّدارةِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَذَلِكَ) أي : أنَّ النَّاسَ ... إلخ، وقوله : (بيان)؛ أي: تبيين الحاجة المحتاج فيه إلى المنطق والمحتاج فيه إلى المنطق هو العصمة، ومحصّله أنَّ العصمة تقع جواباً عن سؤالِ السَّائلِ عنِ الاحتياج لهُ .
So it is known from this that people, in any matter, need logic, and that is the clarification of the need. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'from this') i.e., from this exposition, which is his statement: 'because thought is not correct always, so there is need...' etc. Some have decided this, but what is more in accordance with the commentator's earlier statement: 'And since the clarification of the need leading to the definition of logic...' etc., is that it is said: 'So it is known from this'; i.e., the totality of the author's statement: 'Knowledge, if it is assent to the relation...' up to his statement: 'And error may occur in it', along with the commentator's statement: 'So we need a canon...' etc. (His statement: 'that people') i.e., the answer of 'that'... etc., and it is the safeguarding of the mind from error in thought; i.e., it is known from what preceded the answer to this question depicted by our statement: 'In any matter do people need logic?' Its answer: They need it for the mentioned safeguard. And his statement: 'in any matter' is attached to 'need', and it is given precedence because 'any' is interrogative, so it has the right to precedence. (His statement: 'and that') i.e., what is known from it is the answer, which is the author's statement: 'Knowledge, if it is assent to the relation...' up to the end of his statement: 'And error may occur in it' along with the commentator's statement: 'So we need...' etc. (His statement: 'and that is the clarification of the need') i.e., the mention of that is an explanation, or: that has an explanation of the mentioned safeguard; i.e., affirmation that it is the goal of this science and its benefit. Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'that people...' etc.) The source derived from 'that' along with its clause consisting of its predicate, which is 'they need', is the deputy subject of 'it is known' with the deletion of an added word, and the estimation is: 'the answer to the need of people is known'; i.e., the answer to the question about that. So if it is said: 'In any matter are people need logic?' it is said in answer: 'For the safeguarding of thought from error'. So 'any' here is interrogative, and it and its genitive are attached to 'they need' and are given precedence for fronting. (His statement: 'and that') i.e., 'that people...' etc., and his statement: 'clarification'; i.e., an explanation of the need which is needed for logic, and that which is needed for logic is the safeguard. The summary is that the safeguard occurs as an answer to the questioner's question about the need for it.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's statement about the need for logic. It addresses the question: 'In what do people need logic?' The answer, drawn from earlier statements about the fallibility of thought, is that logic is needed to safeguard the mind from error. Al-Dasuqi clarifies the referents: 'from this' refers to the previous exposition that thought is not always correct, and 'that people' is the answer phrase. He distinguishes between competing interpretations of what 'this' refers to, opting for the totality of the author's definition of knowledge and the commentator's need for a canon. The key point is that the need (al-haja) is for 'isma (safeguard) of the mind from error. Al-'Attar then analyzes the grammatical structure, explaining that 'that people...' is a source clause that functions as the deputy subject, and 'in any matter' is fronted due to the interrogative nature of 'any'. He concludes that the safeguard is the answer to the question about the need. Thus, the passage establishes that the purpose of logic is to protect thought from error, a foundational claim for the science.

The Structure of Acquired Knowledge: Concepts and Assents
Author establishes the foundation that acquired knowledge is derived from necessary knowledge via thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and acquired knowledge and identify the two types of acquired knowledge: concepts and assents.

Core Idea
All acquired knowledge is built from necessary premises through thought, which can err.

This lesson unpacks the opening statement of the matn, which establishes the foundation of the need for logic. All knowledge that is not self-evident is acquired through a process called iktisab. This process yields either concepts (tasawwur) or assents (tasdiq). Understanding this distinction is crucial to see why a safeguard is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of knowledge
The text distinguishes necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). Note that necessary knowledge is immediate, while acquired is derived.
2
Understand the process of acquisition (iktisab)
Acquisition is thought (fikr) and reflection (nazar). For concepts, it is the explicative statement; for assents, it is the syllogism.
3
Recognize the potential for error
Since thought is not always correct, error can enter the acquired knowledge.

Source Text

والكسبيُّ مستفاد من الضَّروري بطريق الاكتساب . وقد يقع في الاكتساب الخطأ؛ لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً ; فَاحْتِيجَ إِلَى قَانُونٍ يَعْصِمُ عَنْهُ ; وَهُوَ المُنْطِقُ.
قَالَ الدسوقي: قوله : والكسبي أي: سواء كان تصوراً أو تصديقاً، وقوله: مستفاد من الضروري؛ أي: تصوراً أو تصديقاً. وقوله: بطريق الاكتساب الإضافة بيانية؛ أي: بطريق هي الاكتساب، وهو الفكر والنظر، وهو القول الشارح بالنسبة للتصور، والقياس بالنسبة للتصديق. وقوله: وقد يقع في الاكتساب؛ يعني: المكتسب من التصور والتصديق. وقوله: لأن الفكر؛ أي: المؤدي إليه.
قوله : فاحتيج إلى قانون القانون : لفظ يوناني معناه في الأصل: القاعدة، وهي قضية كلية يتعرف منها أحكام جزئيات موضوعها. والمراد بالقانون هنا : مجموع قواعد هذا الفن، وتسمية هذا المجموع قانوناً من باب تسمية الشيء باسم بعض أجزائه، وإنما قيل لهذا الفن قانون مع أنه قوانين متعددة؛ لكونها كالواحد من حيث إنها مشتركة في جهة واحدة تجمعها، وهي كونها تعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر.
لا يُقالُ: يمكنُ التَّباعدُ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وحينئذ؛ فلا يحتاج للقانون المذكور ; لأنَّا نقولُ : إنَّ ذلك الخطأ غير معيَّن حتَّى يتباعد عنه، وحينئذ ؛ فيحتاجُ للقانون المذكور.
قوله : يعصم عنه أي: يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ. قوله : وهو أي: القانون الذي يعصم عن الخطأ; (المنطق)، وإنما سمي ذلك القانون بالمنطق ; لأنه يطلق في الأصل على الإدراكات الكلية، وهي نطق باطني . وعلى التلفظ بدال متعلق تلك الإدراكات، وهو نطق ظاهري، وعلى القوة العاقلة التي هي محل صدور تلك الإدراكات. والقانون المذكور به تصيب الإدراكات الكلية، وبه تكون القدرة على التلفظ بدال متعلق الإدراكات الكلية، وبه تتقوى القوة العاقلة وتكمل قوله : (وهو المنطق) وحينئذ ; فقوله: تعريف; أي: دال تعريف المنطق، فهو قانون كلّي تعصم مراعاته الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، وقوله في بيان الحاجة; أي: في تبيين ما يفيد التصديق بالحاجة .
قَالَ العطار: قوله : فاحتيج مفرع على قوله : وقد يقع فيه الخطأ، وقد استشكل تفريعه عليه بأنه لا يلزم من وقوع الخطأ في النظر الجزئي الاحتياج إلى قانون كلي، وذلك لأنه يجوز أن تكفي الفطرة في العصمة، ويكون وقوع الخطأ لعدم إعمالها، ويجوز أن تعرف الأنظار الجزئية من غير معرفة قانون كلي ; فيحترز بتلك المعرفة عن الخطأ، وأجيب : بأن التفريع لظهور عدم كفاية الفطرة; إذ بعد إثبات وقوع الخطأ فيه من الإنسان; لا وجه لكون الفطرة الإنسانية كافية في ذلك التمييز، وإلا ; لم يتصور وقوع الخطأ فيه من صاحبها، فلا حاجة إلى إثبات عدمه، وأما الأنظار الجزئية ; فإنه يتعذر ضبطها ; لتكثرها بتكثر الأزمان، فلا بد من أمر كلي ينطلق عليها.
The acquired (knowledge) is derived from the necessary by way of acquisition. Error may occur in acquisition because thought is not always correct. So a law is needed that safeguards from it, and that is logic.
al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'the acquired' means whether it is a concept or an assent. His statement 'derived from the necessary' means as a concept or an assent. His statement 'by way of acquisition' is an explicative annexation; i.e., by a way that is acquisition, which is thought and reflection, and it is the explicative statement regarding the concept, and the syllogism regarding the assent. His statement 'error may occur in acquisition' means in the acquired from concept and assent. His statement 'because thought' means that which leads to it.
His statement 'so a law is needed': 'law' is a Greek word originally meaning a rule, which is a universal proposition from which are known the judgments of its particular subjects. What is meant by 'law' here is the set of rules of this discipline. Naming this set 'law' is a case of naming something by the name of some of its parts. It is called this discipline 'law' even though it is multiple laws because they are like one in that they share a single aspect that gathers them, which is that they safeguard the mind from error in thought.
It cannot be said: it is possible to avoid error in thought, and then there is no need for the mentioned law; because we say: that error is not definite so as to avoid it, and then the mentioned law is needed.
His statement 'it safeguards from it' means it safeguards the mind from error. His statement 'and it is' means the law that safeguards from error is logic. It is called 'logic' because it is originally applied to universal perceptions, which are inner speech, and to the utterance of an indicator related to those perceptions, which is outer speech, and to the rational faculty which is the locus of issuing those perceptions. By the mentioned law, universal perceptions are attained, and by it the ability to utter an indicator related to universal perceptions is achieved, and by it the rational faculty is strengthened and completed. His statement 'and it is logic' – then his statement is a definition; i.e., it indicates the definition of logic, so it is a universal law, observing which safeguards the mind from error in thought. His statement in explaining the need; i.e., in clarifying what benefits the assent to the need.
al-‘Attar said: His statement 'so a need arose' is branched from his statement 'error may occur in it'. It has been objected to this branching that the occurrence of error in particular thought does not necessitate the need for a universal law, because it is possible that natural disposition (fitra) suffices for protection, and the occurrence of error is due to its not being exercised, and it is possible to know particular thoughts without knowing a universal law, and so one guards against error by that knowledge. It was answered: The branching is due to the apparent insufficiency of natural disposition, for after establishing the occurrence of error in it from man, there is no basis for human natural disposition being sufficient for that discrimination; otherwise, the occurrence of error from its possessor would not be conceivable, so there is no need to prove its absence. As for particular thoughts, they are difficult to regulate due to their multiplicity with the multiplicity of times, so a universal matter that applies to them is necessary.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a front matter discussion justifying the need for the science of logic. It begins by stating that all acquired knowledge (whether conceptual or assent-based) is derived from necessary knowledge through a process of acquisition (iktisab). This acquisition involves thought (fikr) and reflection (nazar). However, because human thought is fallible—it is not always correct—error can occur in this acquired knowledge. Therefore, a universal law (qanun) is needed to safeguard the mind from such error, and that law is logic (mantiq).

The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies each part of the matn. He explains that 'acquired' applies to both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq). The 'way of acquisition' is thought, which for concepts is the 'explicative statement' (al-qawl al-sharih) and for assents is the syllogism (qiyas). Dasuqi then defines 'law' (qanun) as a Greek term originally meaning a rule: a universal proposition from which the judgments of its particular subjects are known. Here it refers to the entire set of rules of logic, called a 'law' by naming the whole after one of its parts, because all these rules share the common function of safeguarding the mind from error in thought.

Dasuqi then addresses a potential objection: could one not simply avoid error in thought without needing such a universal law? He responds that the error is not specific or determinate (ghayr mu‘ayyan), so one cannot simply avoid it without a general law. Thus the law is necessary.

Next, Dasuqi explains why this law is called 'logic' (mantiq). The term 'mantiq' originally refers to three things: (1) universal perceptions (idrakat kulliya), which are inner speech (nutq batini); (2) the utterance of an indicator (dal) related to those perceptions, which is outer speech (nutq zahiri); and (3) the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-‘aqila) which is the locus of those perceptions. The law of logic enables the attainment of universal perceptions, the ability to express them, and the strengthening of the rational faculty. Hence, the definition of logic is: a universal law, the observance of which safeguards the mind from error in thought.

The commentator al-‘Attar presents another objection: the occurrence of error in some particular thought does not necessarily entail the need for a universal law, because a person's innate disposition (fitra) might suffice to avoid error, and one could know particular thoughts without knowing a universal law. The response is that the branching (tafri‘) from the occurrence of error to the need for a law is based on the apparent insufficiency of fitra. If fitra were sufficient, error would not occur at all. Since error does occur, fitra is not sufficient. Moreover, particular thoughts are too numerous to be regulated individually; therefore, a universal principle that governs them all is necessary.

Thus, the passage establishes the rational foundation for the science of logic as a necessary safeguard for the human mind in its pursuit of knowledge.

Key Terms
الكسبي
الضروري
الاكتساب
الفكر
النظر
