Defining Logic as the Universal Safeguarding Law
Author defines the law of logic and explains its nomenclature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'qanun' and 'mantiq' as presented in the text and identify the three aspects of mantiq.

Core Idea
Logic is a universal law whose observance safeguards the mind from error, named after inner speech.

After establishing the need for a law to prevent error, the text defines that law. The term 'qanun' (law) refers to the entire set of rules of logic, which function together to protect the intellect. The term 'mantiq' is derived from inner speech (nutq batini) and encompasses universal perceptions, their expression, and the rational faculty. This lesson unpacks the formal definition of logic.

Guided Path
1
Examine the definition of 'law' (qanun)
The text says a law is a universal proposition from which judgments of particulars are known. Here it refers to the set of all rules of logic.
2
Understand why the set of rules is called a law
It is a naming of the whole by one of its parts, because all rules share the single function of safeguarding the mind from error.
3
Explore the three meanings of mantiq
Logic is named from inner speech: (1) universal perceptions, (2) utterance of indicators, (3) the rational faculty. The science of logic enables all three.
4
Formulate the complete definition
Logic is a universal law, the observance of which safeguards the mind from error in thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكسبيُّ مستفاد من الضَّروري بطريق الاكتساب . وقد يقع في الاكتساب الخطأ؛ لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً ; فَاحْتِيجَ إِلَى قَانُونٍ يَعْصِمُ عَنْهُ ; وَهُوَ المُنْطِقُ.
قَالَ الدسوقي: قوله : والكسبي أي: سواء كان تصوراً أو تصديقاً، وقوله: مستفاد من الضروري؛ أي: تصوراً أو تصديقاً. وقوله: بطريق الاكتساب الإضافة بيانية؛ أي: بطريق هي الاكتساب، وهو الفكر والنظر، وهو القول الشارح بالنسبة للتصور، والقياس بالنسبة للتصديق. وقوله: وقد يقع في الاكتساب؛ يعني: المكتسب من التصور والتصديق. وقوله: لأن الفكر؛ أي: المؤدي إليه.
قوله : فاحتيج إلى قانون القانون : لفظ يوناني معناه في الأصل: القاعدة، وهي قضية كلية يتعرف منها أحكام جزئيات موضوعها. والمراد بالقانون هنا : مجموع قواعد هذا الفن، وتسمية هذا المجموع قانوناً من باب تسمية الشيء باسم بعض أجزائه، وإنما قيل لهذا الفن قانون مع أنه قوانين متعددة؛ لكونها كالواحد من حيث إنها مشتركة في جهة واحدة تجمعها، وهي كونها تعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر.
لا يُقالُ: يمكنُ التَّباعدُ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وحينئذ؛ فلا يحتاج للقانون المذكور ; لأنَّا نقولُ : إنَّ ذلك الخطأ غير معيَّن حتَّى يتباعد عنه، وحينئذ ؛ فيحتاجُ للقانون المذكور.
قوله : يعصم عنه أي: يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ. قوله : وهو أي: القانون الذي يعصم عن الخطأ; (المنطق)، وإنما سمي ذلك القانون بالمنطق ; لأنه يطلق في الأصل على الإدراكات الكلية، وهي نطق باطني . وعلى التلفظ بدال متعلق تلك الإدراكات، وهو نطق ظاهري، وعلى القوة العاقلة التي هي محل صدور تلك الإدراكات. والقانون المذكور به تصيب الإدراكات الكلية، وبه تكون القدرة على التلفظ بدال متعلق الإدراكات الكلية، وبه تتقوى القوة العاقلة وتكمل قوله : (وهو المنطق) وحينئذ ; فقوله: تعريف; أي: دال تعريف المنطق، فهو قانون كلّي تعصم مراعاته الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، وقوله في بيان الحاجة; أي: في تبيين ما يفيد التصديق بالحاجة .
قَالَ العطار: قوله : فاحتيج مفرع على قوله : وقد يقع فيه الخطأ، وقد استشكل تفريعه عليه بأنه لا يلزم من وقوع الخطأ في النظر الجزئي الاحتياج إلى قانون كلي، وذلك لأنه يجوز أن تكفي الفطرة في العصمة، ويكون وقوع الخطأ لعدم إعمالها، ويجوز أن تعرف الأنظار الجزئية من غير معرفة قانون كلي ; فيحترز بتلك المعرفة عن الخطأ، وأجيب : بأن التفريع لظهور عدم كفاية الفطرة; إذ بعد إثبات وقوع الخطأ فيه من الإنسان; لا وجه لكون الفطرة الإنسانية كافية في ذلك التمييز، وإلا ; لم يتصور وقوع الخطأ فيه من صاحبها، فلا حاجة إلى إثبات عدمه، وأما الأنظار الجزئية ; فإنه يتعذر ضبطها ; لتكثرها بتكثر الأزمان، فلا بد من أمر كلي ينطلق عليها.
The acquired (knowledge) is derived from the necessary by way of acquisition. Error may occur in acquisition because thought is not always correct. So a law is needed that safeguards from it, and that is logic.
al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'the acquired' means whether it is a concept or an assent. His statement 'derived from the necessary' means as a concept or an assent. His statement 'by way of acquisition' is an explicative annexation; i.e., by a way that is acquisition, which is thought and reflection, and it is the explicative statement regarding the concept, and the syllogism regarding the assent. His statement 'error may occur in acquisition' means in the acquired from concept and assent. His statement 'because thought' means that which leads to it.
His statement 'so a law is needed': 'law' is a Greek word originally meaning a rule, which is a universal proposition from which are known the judgments of its particular subjects. What is meant by 'law' here is the set of rules of this discipline. Naming this set 'law' is a case of naming something by the name of some of its parts. It is called this discipline 'law' even though it is multiple laws because they are like one in that they share a single aspect that gathers them, which is that they safeguard the mind from error in thought.
It cannot be said: it is possible to avoid error in thought, and then there is no need for the mentioned law; because we say: that error is not definite so as to avoid it, and then the mentioned law is needed.
His statement 'it safeguards from it' means it safeguards the mind from error. His statement 'and it is' means the law that safeguards from error is logic. It is called 'logic' because it is originally applied to universal perceptions, which are inner speech, and to the utterance of an indicator related to those perceptions, which is outer speech, and to the rational faculty which is the locus of issuing those perceptions. By the mentioned law, universal perceptions are attained, and by it the ability to utter an indicator related to universal perceptions is achieved, and by it the rational faculty is strengthened and completed. His statement 'and it is logic' – then his statement is a definition; i.e., it indicates the definition of logic, so it is a universal law, observing which safeguards the mind from error in thought. His statement in explaining the need; i.e., in clarifying what benefits the assent to the need.
al-‘Attar said: His statement 'so a need arose' is branched from his statement 'error may occur in it'. It has been objected to this branching that the occurrence of error in particular thought does not necessitate the need for a universal law, because it is possible that natural disposition (fitra) suffices for protection, and the occurrence of error is due to its not being exercised, and it is possible to know particular thoughts without knowing a universal law, and so one guards against error by that knowledge. It was answered: The branching is due to the apparent insufficiency of natural disposition, for after establishing the occurrence of error in it from man, there is no basis for human natural disposition being sufficient for that discrimination; otherwise, the occurrence of error from its possessor would not be conceivable, so there is no need to prove its absence. As for particular thoughts, they are difficult to regulate due to their multiplicity with the multiplicity of times, so a universal matter that applies to them is necessary.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a front matter discussion justifying the need for the science of logic. It begins by stating that all acquired knowledge (whether conceptual or assent-based) is derived from necessary knowledge through a process of acquisition (iktisab). This acquisition involves thought (fikr) and reflection (nazar). However, because human thought is fallible—it is not always correct—error can occur in this acquired knowledge. Therefore, a universal law (qanun) is needed to safeguard the mind from such error, and that law is logic (mantiq).

The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies each part of the matn. He explains that 'acquired' applies to both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq). The 'way of acquisition' is thought, which for concepts is the 'explicative statement' (al-qawl al-sharih) and for assents is the syllogism (qiyas). Dasuqi then defines 'law' (qanun) as a Greek term originally meaning a rule: a universal proposition from which the judgments of its particular subjects are known. Here it refers to the entire set of rules of logic, called a 'law' by naming the whole after one of its parts, because all these rules share the common function of safeguarding the mind from error in thought.

Dasuqi then addresses a potential objection: could one not simply avoid error in thought without needing such a universal law? He responds that the error is not specific or determinate (ghayr mu‘ayyan), so one cannot simply avoid it without a general law. Thus the law is necessary.

Next, Dasuqi explains why this law is called 'logic' (mantiq). The term 'mantiq' originally refers to three things: (1) universal perceptions (idrakat kulliya), which are inner speech (nutq batini); (2) the utterance of an indicator (dal) related to those perceptions, which is outer speech (nutq zahiri); and (3) the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-‘aqila) which is the locus of those perceptions. The law of logic enables the attainment of universal perceptions, the ability to express them, and the strengthening of the rational faculty. Hence, the definition of logic is: a universal law, the observance of which safeguards the mind from error in thought.

The commentator al-‘Attar presents another objection: the occurrence of error in some particular thought does not necessarily entail the need for a universal law, because a person's innate disposition (fitra) might suffice to avoid error, and one could know particular thoughts without knowing a universal law. The response is that the branching (tafri‘) from the occurrence of error to the need for a law is based on the apparent insufficiency of fitra. If fitra were sufficient, error would not occur at all. Since error does occur, fitra is not sufficient. Moreover, particular thoughts are too numerous to be regulated individually; therefore, a universal principle that governs them all is necessary.

Thus, the passage establishes the rational foundation for the science of logic as a necessary safeguard for the human mind in its pursuit of knowledge.

Key Terms
القانون
المنطق
النطق الباطني
النطق الظاهري
القوة العاقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dasuqi, why is the set of logical rules called a 'law' (qanun)?
A) Because logic is derived from Greek philosophy.
B) Because all rules share the common aspect of safeguarding the mind from error.
C) Because logic is a single rule.
D) Because the term 'law' is used in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Logic is defined as a universal law that safeguards the mind from error, and its name derives from its connection to inner speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition of logic as 'a universal law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought', identify which of the following is NOT part of the genus: (a) universal law, (b) set of rules, (c) a single rule. Explain your answer.
?
List the three meanings of nutq (speech) mentioned in the text and give an example of each from Islamic scholarship.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
Defending the Necessity of Logic: The Insufficiency of Fitra
Author refutes the objection that fitra or particular knowledge suffices to avoid error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against the need for a universal law and explain the response that fitra and particular knowledge are insufficient.

Core Idea
Fitrah alone cannot prevent error, and particular thoughts are too numerous to regulate without a universal law.

Having argued for the necessity of logic, the text anticipates and refutes an objection. The objector suggests that one could avoid error by using natural disposition (fitra) or by knowing particular rules for each case. The response demonstrates why these alternatives fail, reinforcing the indispensability of a universal law. This lesson examines that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that error can be avoided without a universal law, either by fitra or by knowing particular thoughts.
2
Analyze the response regarding fitra
The response argues that if fitra were sufficient, error would never occur. Since error does occur, fitra is not enough.
3
Analyze the response regarding particular thoughts
Particular thoughts are too numerous across time to be known individually; thus a universal principle covering them all is needed.
4
Evaluate the strength of the defense
Consider whether the objector's alternative is realistically viable. The text's reasoning shows that neither fitra nor particular knowledge can substitute for a universal law.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a front matter discussion justifying the need for the science of logic. It begins by stating that all acquired knowledge (whether conceptual or assent-based) is derived from necessary knowledge through a process of acquisition (iktisab). This acquisition involves thought (fikr) and reflection (nazar). However, because human thought is fallible—it is not always correct—error can occur in this acquired knowledge. Therefore, a universal law (qanun) is needed to safeguard the mind from such error, and that law is logic (mantiq).

The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies each part of the matn. He explains that 'acquired' applies to both concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq). The 'way of acquisition' is thought, which for concepts is the 'explicative statement' (al-qawl al-sharih) and for assents is the syllogism (qiyas). Dasuqi then defines 'law' (qanun) as a Greek term originally meaning a rule: a universal proposition from which the judgments of its particular subjects are known. Here it refers to the entire set of rules of logic, called a 'law' by naming the whole after one of its parts, because all these rules share the common function of safeguarding the mind from error in thought.

Dasuqi then addresses a potential objection: could one not simply avoid error in thought without needing such a universal law? He responds that the error is not specific or determinate (ghayr mu‘ayyan), so one cannot simply avoid it without a general law. Thus the law is necessary.

Next, Dasuqi explains why this law is called 'logic' (mantiq). The term 'mantiq' originally refers to three things: (1) universal perceptions (idrakat kulliya), which are inner speech (nutq batini); (2) the utterance of an indicator (dal) related to those perceptions, which is outer speech (nutq zahiri); and (3) the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-‘aqila) which is the locus of those perceptions. The law of logic enables the attainment of universal perceptions, the ability to express them, and the strengthening of the rational faculty. Hence, the definition of logic is: a universal law, the observance of which safeguards the mind from error in thought.

The commentator al-‘Attar presents another objection: the occurrence of error in some particular thought does not necessarily entail the need for a universal law, because a person's innate disposition (fitra) might suffice to avoid error, and one could know particular thoughts without knowing a universal law. The response is that the branching (tafri‘) from the occurrence of error to the need for a law is based on the apparent insufficiency of fitra. If fitra were sufficient, error would not occur at all. Since error does occur, fitra is not sufficient. Moreover, particular thoughts are too numerous to be regulated individually; therefore, a universal principle that governs them all is necessary.

Thus, the passage establishes the rational foundation for the science of logic as a necessary safeguard for the human mind in its pursuit of knowledge.

According to al-‘Attar, why cannot particular thoughts (anẓar juz’iyya) replace a universal law?
A) Because they are too few to be useful.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Necessity of Knowing Benefit and Definition Before Studying a Science
Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar argue for prerequisites of studying a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a student must know a science's benefit and definition before studying it with insight, and distinguish between mere commencement and insightful commencement.

Core Idea
Voluntary acts require knowledge of benefit; insightful study requires definition of the science.

Why do we need to know the benefit and definition of a science before starting to study it? The scholars of logic and method argue that commencing a science is a voluntary act, and every voluntary act requires the agent to know its benefit. Without that, the act is futile. Moreover, to study with insight (baṣīra), one must also have a conceptualization of the science through its definition. This passage from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar unpacks these prerequisites using the example of a carpenter building a bed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Notice that the passage begins by stating that commencing a science is a voluntary act (fiʿl ikhtiyārī). This is the foundational claim. Why is it important? Because voluntary acts are not done without purpose.
The passage explicitly says: 'Commencing a science is a voluntary act.'
2
Apply the condition for voluntary acts
The author argues that a voluntary act requires the agent to know its benefit beforehand. He illustrates with the carpenter who does not make a bed unless he knows he will sit on it. So, a student must believe the science has a benefit before starting. Otherwise, the act is futile (ʿabath).
The benefit must also be significant relative to the effort involved.
3
Distinguish mere commencement from insightful commencement
Al-Dasuqi differentiates between two levels: 'mere commencement' (aṣl al-shurūʿ) only requires a vague concept of the science (e.g., that it is one of the sciences). But 'commencement with insight' (al-shurūʿ ʿalā baṣīra) requires conceptualization by a definition (rasm). Without that, the student lacks insight and his effort may become futile.
The definition here is a descriptive definition (rasm), not necessarily a full hadd.
4
Examine the role of definition in providing insight
Al-Attar elaborates: if the student knows the actual benefit of the science, his desire is perfected and he perseveres. If he later discovers the benefit after starting, his initial striving may become futile, proving he was not upon insight. Thus, definition and benefit are prerequisites for insightful study.
The passage also addresses an objection using conditional reasoning: if the benefit is not known beforehand, effort may become futile.

وعلى تعريف العِلْم ؛ لأنَّه لو لم يَتَصوَّرُ ذلكَ العِلْمَ أَوَّلاً ؛ لَمَا كَانَ على بصيرة في طلبه، الدسوقي والحاصلُ : أنَّ الشروعَ في العِلْم فعل اختياري، والفعلُ الاختياري لا يصدرُ من الفاعل المختار إلا بعد أن يصدِّقُ بأنَّ فائدته كذا، فالنجارُ لا يفعل سريراً إلا بعد عِلْمِهِ أَنَّهُ يجلس عليه، وحينئذ ؛ فَلَا بُدَّ أن يعتقد الشَّارعُ في العِلْم قبل شروعه أنَّ لِذلكَ العِلْم فائدةٌ، وإلا ، كان شروعُه عبثاً، ولا بُدَّ أن تكون تلك الفائدة مُعتدا بها عندَهُ بالنَّظَرِ للمشقَّةِ الحاصلة للمشتغل بذلكَ العِلْمِ؛ كَانَ مُعتدا بِهَا في الواقعِ أَوْ لَا ، وَإِلَّا ؛ كانَ شروعُهُ فِيهِ يُعَدُّ عبثاً . (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَتَصَوَّرُ ذَلِكَ) أي : فَلِأنَّ الشَّارعَ لو لم يتصوَّرُ ذلكَ العِلْمَ برسمه؛ أي رسم كان وقوله : (أولاً)؛ أي: قبل الشروع فيه، وقوله : (لما كان على بصيرةٍ)؛ أي: تبصُّر ومعرفةٍ في طلبه، وحينئذ ؛ فيكون شروعُه على وجهِ البصيرة متوقفاً على تَصوُّرِهِ برسمه، وأمَّا أصلُ الشُّروعِ فَلَا يتوقَّفُ على ذلك بلْ على تَصوُّرِهِ بوجةٍ مَا ؛ كَكَونِهِ عِلماً من العلوم . العطار و مزيدُ اختصاص به، بأنْ يكونَ تَدوينه لأجلِهَا ؛ لَكَانَ طلبه عبثاً، وهذا كلامٌ مُجْمَلٌ، تفصيلُهُ مَا قالَهُ السَّيّدُ أنَّ الشُّروعَ في العِلْم فِعْل اختياريُّ، فَلَا بُدَّ أن يعلمَ أَوَّلاً أَنَّ لذلكَ العِلْم فائدةً ما ، وإلا ؛ لامتنعَ الشُّروعُ فِيهِ كَمَا بَيَّنَ في موضعِهِ، ولا بُدَّ أن تكونَ تِلكَ الفائدةُ مُعتَدًّا بِهَا بِالنَّظَرِ إلى المشقَّةِ الَّتي تَكونُ في تحصيل ذلكَ العِلْم، وإلا ، لَكَانَ شروعُهُ فيه وطلبُهُ لَهُ يُعَدُّ عَبَثاً عُرْفاً ، وبذلك يفترُ جَدُّهُ قطعاً، ولَا بُدَّ أَن تكون الفائدة هي الفائدة الَّتي تَتَرَتَّب على ذلكَ العِلْمِ؛ إذْ لَو لَمْ تَكُنْ إِيَّاهَا ؛ لَرَبَّما زالَ اعتقادُهُ بعدَ الشُّروعِ فِيهِ لِعَدَمِ المناسبة بينَهُمَا ، فَيَصيرُ سَعِيهُ فِي طَلَبِهِ عَبَثاً، وَفِي نَظَرِهِ ضَلَالاً، وأمَّا إذا عَلِمَ الفائدة المعتد بها المترتبة عليه؛ فإِنَّهُ تتكمَّل رغبته فيهِ، ويبالغ في تحصيله كما هو حقه، ويزداد ذلك الاعتناء بعدَ الشُّروع بواسطة مناسبتِهِ لِتلكَ الفائدة .ا.هـ . لا يُقَالُ : يجوز أن يعتقد بعد زوالِ الاعتقادِ الأَوَّلِ فائدتَهُ المترتبة عليه، وتكونُ مهمَّةً لَهُ؛ فَيسعى في تحصيله لأجل هذهِ الفائدةِ، فَلَا يَصيرُ سَعْيُهُ السَّابِقُ عَبَثاً؛ لِأَنَّا نقولُ : هذا لا يضرُّ ؛ لأنَّ قولَهُ فيصيرُ أيضاً داخل تحتَ ربَّما ، وإذا صارَ سَعيُهُ السَّابِقُ عَبَثاً ؛ عَلِمَ أَنَّهُ لمْ يَكُنْ على بصيرةٍ في شُروعِهِ، وقَوْلُ المحشّي بعد أنْ ذَكَرَ بَعضاً مِمَّا ذَكرنَاهُ عن السَّيْدِ : وبِهِ تعلمُ مَا في كلامِ الشَّارِحِ؛ أرادَ بهِ الإجمالَ الَّذي فَصَّلْنَاهُ، وقَوْلُ مَن تعقَّبَهُ بعدَ أنْ لَفَّقَ كَلاماً مِنَ السَّيِّدِ وعَبْدِ الحكيمِ، وَبِمَا حَرَّرْنَاهُ عِندَ التَّدبرِ ؛ يُظهِرُ أَنَّ كلامَ الشَّارحِ ليس فيه شيءٌ ا.هـ. ، ليس بشيء، ولستُ أدري أيَّ شَيءٍ حرَّرَهُ، بَل مَا ذَكَرَهُ مُجرَّدُ تلفيق .
And upon the definition of the science; because if he does not conceptualize that science first, he would not be upon insight in seeking it. Al-Dasuqi: The summary is that commencing a science is a voluntary act, and a voluntary act is not issued from the free agent except after he affirms that its benefit is such-and-such. For the carpenter does not make a bed except after knowing that he will sit on it. Therefore, it is necessary that the one commencing a science believes before his commencement that that science has a benefit; otherwise his commencement would be futile. And it is necessary that that benefit be considered significant by him in view of the difficulty incurred by the one occupied with that science, whether it is significant in reality or not; otherwise his commencement in it would be considered futile. (His statement 'because if he does not conceptualize that') means that the commencer, if he does not conceptualize that science by its definition—whatever definition—and his statement 'first' means before commencing it, and his statement 'he would not be upon insight' means he would not have perception and knowledge in his seeking of it. Then, his commencement in the manner of insight is dependent on his conceptualization of it by its definition. As for the mere commencement, it is not dependent on that, but rather on his conceptualization of it in some manner, such as it being one of the sciences. Al-Attar: And more specificity to it, that its compilation be for its sake; otherwise seeking it would be futile. This is a summary statement, its detail is what al-Sayyid said: that commencing a science is a voluntary act, so he must first know that that science has some benefit, otherwise commencement in it is impossible as explained in its place. And it is necessary that that benefit be considered significant in view of the difficulty that is in acquiring that science, otherwise his commencement in it and his seeking it would be considered futile by custom, and his earnestness would certainly slacken. And it is necessary that the benefit be the benefit that results from that science; because if it is not that benefit, perhaps his belief would disappear after commencing it due to the lack of suitability between them, so his striving in seeking it becomes futile and his effort becomes misguidance. But if he knows the significant benefit resulting from it, then his desire for it is perfected, and he goes to great lengths in acquiring it as it deserves, and that attention increases after commencement by virtue of its suitability for that benefit. End. It cannot be said: It is permissible that after the first belief disappears, he believes its resulting benefit and it becomes important to him, so he strives to acquire it for this benefit, so his previous striving does not become futile. Because we say: This does not harm; because his statement 'becomes' also falls under 'perhaps', and if his previous striving becomes futile, he knows that he was not upon insight in his commencement. And the statement of the glossator after he mentioned some of what we mentioned from al-Sayyid: 'And by it you know what is in the commentator's speech'—he intended by it the summary which we have detailed. And the statement of one who criticized him after piecing together speech from al-Sayyid and Abd al-Hakim, and by our checking when contemplating, it shows that the commentator's speech has nothing in it, end—it is nothing, and I do not know what he checked; rather what he mentioned is mere patchwork.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar on a statement in the matn that knowledge of a science's definition is necessary for insightful study. They begin by asserting that commencing a science is a voluntary act. Every voluntary act requires that the agent knows its benefit; otherwise the act is futile (عبث). They illustrate with the example of a carpenter who builds a bed only after knowing he will sit on it. Therefore, a student must believe that the science has a benefit before starting. Moreover, the benefit must be considered significant relative to the effort required; otherwise the commencement is futile. Al-Dasuqi then distinguishes between two levels: mere commencement (أصل الشروع), which only requires some vague conceptualization (e.g., knowing it is a science), and insightful commencement (الشروع على بصيرة), which requires conceptualization by definition (رسم). Al-Attar elaborates: he explains that the student must know the actual benefit that results from the science, else he may lose interest after starting. He also addresses an objection: what if one later discovers the benefit after initially starting? The response is that if the initial belief in the benefit disappears, the initial striving becomes futile, proving he was not upon insight. Finally, al-Attar criticizes a glossator who tried to find fault with the commentator's speech, dismissing it as mere patchwork. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that proper commencement requires both knowledge of the benefit and a proper definition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the example of the carpenter mentioned?
A) To show that a carpenter must be skilled in his craft.
B) To illustrate that voluntary acts require knowledge of the benefit before action.
C) To emphasize that a science must have a practical benefit.
D) To argue that definition is unnecessary for beginning a science.

Key Takeaway
Knowing a science's benefit prevents futility; knowing its definition provides insight for successful study.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the stated benefit of studying 'Ilm al-Kalam (theology) as given in a classic text like al-Sanusiyya. How does knowing that benefit help a student study with insight?
?
Distinguish between 'mere commencement' and 'commencement with insight' in your own study of a new Islamic science. What definition (rasm) would you need to have?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
From Definition to Universal Premise: Classifying Issues of Logic
Commentary explains how the definition (rasm) of logic provides a universal premise for classifying its issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism from a definition (rasm) to classify issues of a science, using the example of logic.

Core Idea
A definition yields a universal premise that turns classification into a deductive operation.

The commentators show that the definition of logic is not just for conceptualization—it provides the major premise for a syllogism that decides whether any given issue belongs to logic. This is how 'ilm ijmail (general knowledge) becomes operational.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition as a source of a universal premise
The rasm of logic states that it is a lawful instrument that safeguards the mind from error. From this, a general statement about all its issues can be derived.
The definition is 'آلة قانونية تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر'.
2
Derive the first universal premise
From the definition, we obtain: 'Every issue of logic has an input in the safeguard against error.' This is the immediate universal premise (المقدمة الكلية).
The text says: 'حصل عنده مقدمة كلية، وهي أن كل مسألة من مسائل المنطق لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة'.
3
Derive the second universal premise by implication
The first premise logically implies: 'Every issue that has an input in the safeguard is from logic.' This becomes the major premise for the syllogism.
This is captured in: 'وهذه المقدمة يلزمها مقدمة أخرى، وهي أن كل مسألة لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة فهي من المنطق'.
4
Construct the categorical syllogism for classification
For a specific issue, assume it has input in the safeguard. Then the syllogism runs: All issues with input in the safeguard are from logic; this issue has input; therefore this issue is from logic. For a negative case, the conclusion rejects membership.
The text gives both positive and negative examples: positive: 'هذه المسألة لها دخل في تلك العصمة... تنتج أن هذه المسألة من المنطق'; negative: 'قلت: هذه المسألة ليس لها دخل... فليست من المنطق'.
5
Recognize the epistemic status: ability (tamakkun) not actual knowledge
The commentators stress that this yields the ability (تمكن) to classify, not automatic actual knowledge. One must still construct the syllogism explicitly.
The text says: 'وليس المراد أنه بمجرد ورود تلك المسألة عليه يعلم بالفعل أنها منه بدون تأمل وقياس'.

مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ وإِذا تَصَوَّرَه برَسْمِه ؛ حَصَلَ له العِلْمُ الإجمالي بمسائل ذلكَ العِلْمِ، حتَّى
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِذَا تَصَوَّرَهُ . . . إِلَخ) هذا زيادة فائدة لا بيان لوجهِ التَّوجه .
(قَوْلُهُ : حَصَلَ لَهُ الْعِلْمُ الْإِجْمَالِيُّ) أي: وذلكَ لأَنَّ مَن تصوَّرَ المنطق بأنَّهُ آلةٌ قانونيَّةٌ تعصمُ مراعاتُها الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، وعرف أنَّ هذا تعريفه؛ حصل عنده مقدّمة كلّيَّةٌ، وهي أنَّ كُلَّ مَسألةٍ من مسائل المنطق لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة، وهذه المقدّمةُ يلزمها مُقدِّمةٌ أُخرى ، وهي أنَّ كُلَّ مسألة لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة؛ فهي مِنَ المنطق، وبذلك يتمكَّنُ مِنْ أَنْ يعلمَ كُلَّ مَسألةٍ ورَدَتْ عليهِ أنَّها مِنَ المنطقِ أَوْ لَيْسَتْ منهُ تَمكَّنَاً تاماً؛ لأنَّهُ إذا كان لتلك المسألة الواردة عليه مدخل في تلك العصمةِ ؛ قالَ : هذه المسألة لها مدخل في العصمةِ المذكورة، ثمَّ تأخذُ المقدِّمة اللازمة للمقدّمة الحاصلة عندَهُ مِن تَصوُّرِ المنطق برسمه، ومعرفة أنَّ هذا الرَّسم تعريفه ; فَتجعلها كُبرى بأن تقول : هذه المسألة لها دخل في تلك العصمة،
العطار
زالَ اعتقادُهُ بعدَ الشُّروعِ فِيهِ لِعَدَمِ المناسبة بينَهُمَا ، فَيَصيرُ سَعِيهُ فِي طَلَبِهِ عَبَثاً، وَفِي نَظَرِهِ ضَلَالاً، وأمَّا إذا عَلِمَ الفائدة المعتد بها المترتبة عليه؛ فإِنَّهُ تتكمَّل رغبته فيهِ، ويبالغ في تحصيله كما هو حقه، ويزداد ذلك الاعتناء بعدَ الشُّروع بواسطة مناسبتِهِ لِتلكَ الفائدة .ا.هـ . لا يُقَالُ : يجوز أن يعتقد بعد زوالِ الاعتقادِ الأَوَّلِ فائدتَهُ المترتبة عليه، وتكونُ مهمَّةً لَهُ؛ فَيسعى في تحصيله لأجل هذهِ الفائدةِ، فَلَا يَصيرُ سَعْيُهُ السَّابِقُ عَبَثاً؛ لِأَنَّا نقولُ : هذا لا يضرُّ ؛ لأنَّ قولَهُ فيصيرُ أيضاً داخل تحتَ ربَّما ، وإذا صارَ سَعيُهُ السَّابِقُ عَبَثاً ; عَلِمَ أَنَّهُ لمْ يَكُنْ على بصيرةٍ في شُروعِهِ، وقَوْلُ المحشّي بعد أنْ ذَكَرَ بَعضاً مِمَّا ذَكرنَاهُ عن السَّيْدِ : وبِهِ تعلمُ مَا في كلامِ الشَّارِحِ؛ أرادَ بهِ الإجمالَ الَّذي فَصَّلْنَاهُ، وقَوْلُ مَن تعقَّبَهُ بعدَ أنْ لَفَّقَ كَلاماً مِنَ السَّيِّدِ وعَبْدِ الحكيمِ، وَبِمَا حَرَّرْنَاهُ عِندَ التَّدبرِ ; يُظهِرُ أَنَّ كلامَ الشَّارحِ ليس فيه شيءٌ ا.هـ. ، ليس بشيء، ولستُ أدري أيَّ شَيءٍ حرَّرَهُ، بَل مَا ذَكَرَهُ مُجرَّدُ تلفيق .
(قَوْلُهُ: حَصَلَ لَهُ الْعِلْمُ الْإِجْمَالِيُّ حُصولاً بالقوَّةِ القريبةِ مِنَ الفعل، فإنَّ من تصوَّرَ المنطق بأنَّهُ آلةٌ قانونية ... إلخ ; حصلَ عندَهُ مُقدِّمَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، هي أنَّ كُلَّ مَسألة

المنطقية
إنَّ كلَّ مسألة من هذا العِلْمِ تَرِدُ عليه يَعلَمُ أَنَّهَا
الدسوقي
منه
وكلُّ مسألة لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة؛ فهي من المنطق، ينتج أنَّ هذه المسألة مِنَ المنطق، وإن لم تكن المسألة الواردة عليكَ لَها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة. قُلْتُ : هذه المسألةَ ليس لها مدخل في العصمة المذكورة، وكلُّ مسألةٍ كَذلِكَ،
فليسَتْ مِنَ المنطق، ينتجُ : هذه المسألة ليسَتْ مِنَ المنطقِ .
إذا عَلِمْتَ هذا ; فقولُ الشَّارح: (وإذا تصوّره برسمه)؛ أي: بأن تصوّره بأنَّهُ آلةٌ قانونيَّةٌ تعصمُ مُراعاتُهَا الزّهنَ عَن الخطأ، والحالُ أَنَّهُ عارفٌ أَنَّ ذلك تعريف للمنطق، وقوله : (حصلَ لَهُ العِلْمُ ... إلخ)؛ هو العِلْمُ بالمقدِّمة الحاصلة من تصورِ العِلْم برسمه، ومعرفةُ أَنَّهُ تَعريفه، وهي القائلةُ : كلُّ مَسألة من مسائل المنطق لها دخل في العصمة المذكورة، وقوله : ( حتَّى إن ... إلخ) غاية؛ لقوله : حصل له العِلْمُ . . . إلخ، وقولُه : علم أنَّها ؛ أي: تلك المسألة الواردة عليه منه؛ أي: من ذلك العِلم، والمراد بقوله : (علم أنَّها منه ; تمَكُنْ مِنْ عِلْمٍ أَنَّها منه تمكناً تاما، بأن يأتي بالقياس السابق المنتج ; لأنَّها منه، وحينئذ ; يعلم أنَّها منه، وَلَمَّا كانَ هذا التَّمْكُنُ تامَّا قويًّا; عبّر عنه بالعِلم، وليس المرادُ أنَّه بمجرَّدِ ورودِ تلك المسألة
عليه ; يعلمُ بالفعل أنَّها منه بدونِ تأمل وقياس; لأنَّ هذا خلافُ الواقع.
العطار
مِنْهُ لَهَا مَدْخَلٌ فِي تِلْكَ العِصمةِ، ويُمكِن بسبب معرفةِ تِلكَ المقدَّمَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ مسائله وتمييزها عن غيرها تَمكُناً تاما، فإذا وردّ عليهِ مسألةٌ مُعيَّنَةٌ لَهَا مَدخل في تلكَ العِصمةِ; تَمَكَّنَ مِن أنْ يعلم أنَّها مِنَ المنطقِ ; لِوُجودِ قِيَاسِ عِندَهُ; هُوَ أَنَّ هذهِ المسألةَ لها مَدخَلٌ في العصمةِ عن الخطأ في الفكر، وكُلُّ مَسألةٍ كَذلكَ; فَهِيَ مِنَ
المنطق، فهذه المسألةُ مِنَ المنطقِ، وَقِش على ذلك بقيَّةَ العُلُوم. (قَوْلُهُ : يَعْلَمُ أَنَّهَا مِنْهُ) أي: تمكَّنَ مِن علمها تمكُناً تاما بواسطة المقدِّمةِ الَّتي حصَّلَها مِنَ التّعريف فينتظم عندَهُ القياسُ السَّابِقُ، قال عبد الحكيم : والتَّمكُنُ المذكورُ لا يُنافي عدم حصولِ التَّمييز بالفعل في بعض المسائل، كما أنَّ التَّمْكُنَ مِنَ الاجتهادِ لا يُنافي وقوع «لا أدري» في بعض المسائل مِنَ المجتهد، وهذا التَّمَكِّنُ
مَنشَؤُهُ كَوْنُ التّعريفِ مَأخوذاً من جهة الوحدة التي تشترك فيها جميعُ المسائل .
Introduction of the author: And if he conceives it by its description, he obtains general knowledge of the issues of that science, even al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And if he conceives it... etc.') This is an additional benefit, not an explanation of the direction. (His statement: 'He obtains general knowledge') Meaning: that is because whoever conceives of logic as a lawful instrument whose observance safeguards the intellect from error in thought, and knows that this is its definition, there occurs to him a universal premise, which is that every issue of the issues of logic has an input in the mentioned safeguard, and this premise necessitates another premise, which is that every issue that has an input in the mentioned safeguard is from logic, and thereby he becomes able to know every issue that comes to him, whether it is from logic or not, with complete ability; because if that issue coming to him has an input in that safeguard, he says: this issue has an input in the mentioned safeguard, then he takes the necessary premise from the premise obtained by him from conceiving logic by its description, and knowing that this description is its definition, and makes it a major premise by saying: this issue has an input in that safeguard, al-Attar: His belief departed after starting in it due to lack of appropriateness between them, so his effort in seeking it becomes futile, and in his view it is error. But if he knows the due benefit resulting from it, then his desire for it becomes complete, and he strives in acquiring it as is its right, and that care increases after starting through its appropriateness to that benefit. End. It cannot be said: it is permissible that after the removal of the first belief, he believes in its resulting benefit, and it is important to him, so he strives in acquiring it for the sake of this benefit, so his previous effort does not become futile; because we say: this does no harm; because his statement 'becomes' also falls under 'perhaps', and if his previous effort becomes futile, he knows that he was not upon insight in his starting. And the statement of the marginal commentator after he mentioned some of what we mentioned from al-Sayyid: 'By it you know what is in the commentator's statement'—he intended by it the summary that we detailed. And the statement of one who followed him after he compiled words from al-Sayyid and Abd al-Hakim, and by what we verified upon reflection, shows that there is nothing in the commentator's statement. It is nothing, and I do not know what he verified, rather what he mentioned is mere compilation. (His statement: 'He obtains general knowledge') Attainment in potentiality close to actuality. For whoever conceives of logic as a lawful instrument... etc.; there occurs to him a universal premise, which is that every issue [PAGE_172] logic: that every issue of this science that comes to him, he knows that it is al-Dasuqi: from it, and every issue that has an input in the mentioned safeguard is from logic, it results that this issue is from logic, and if the issue coming to you does not have an input in the mentioned safeguard, you say: this issue has no input in the mentioned safeguard, and every such issue, so it is not from logic, it results: this issue is not from logic. If you know this, then the statement of the commentator: 'And if he conceives it by its description' means: by conceiving it as a lawful instrument whose observance safeguards the intellect from error, while he knows that that is the definition of logic. And his statement: 'He obtains knowledge' etc. is the knowledge of the premise obtained from conceiving the science by its description and knowing that it is its definition, which says: every issue of the issues of logic has an input in the mentioned safeguard. And his statement: 'Even' etc. is a limit for his statement: 'He obtains knowledge' etc. And his statement: 'He knows that it is' meaning: that issue coming to him is from it, i.e., from that science, and what is meant by his statement: 'He knows that it is from it' is ability to know it is from it with complete ability, by bringing the previous producing syllogism because it is from it, and then he knows it is from it. And since this ability is complete and strong, he expressed it as knowledge. And the intended meaning is not that merely by the coming of that issue to him, he actually knows it is from it without reflection and syllogism, because that contradicts reality. al-Attar: from it, it has an input in that safeguard, and it is possible through knowing that universal premise to know its issues and distinguish them from others with complete ability. So if a specific issue that has an input in that safeguard comes to him, he is able to know that it is from logic because he has a syllogism: this issue has an input in the safeguard against error in thought, and every such issue is from logic, so this issue is from logic. And analogize to that the rest of the sciences. (His statement: 'He knows that it is from it') means: He is able to know it with complete ability by means of the premise he obtained from the definition, so the previous syllogism is arranged for him. Abd al-Hakim said: The mentioned ability does not negate the non-occurrence of actual distinction in some issues, just as the ability of ijtihad does not negate the occurrence of 'I do not know' in some issues from the mujtahid. And this ability originates from the definition being taken from the aspect of the unity in which all issues share.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh and hashiya) on the author's introductory statement: 'If he conceives it by its description, he obtains general knowledge of that science's issues, even...' The commentators (al-Dasuqi, al-Attar) explain the meaning and extend the idea. The passage opens with al-Dasuqi noting that the author's addition is a benefit, not a clarification of the direction. Then he unpacks: 'general knowledge' means that one who conceives logic by its famous description—'a lawful instrument whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thinking'—and knows that this is the definition, obtains a universal premise: 'Every issue of logic has an input in the mentioned safeguard.' From this necessarily follows another premise: 'Every issue that has an input in the safeguard is from logic.' With these two premises, when a particular issue arises, one can syllogistically determine whether it belongs to logic. For example, if an issue has input in the safeguard, one says: 'This issue has input in the safeguard; every such issue is from logic; therefore, this issue is from logic.' If it lacks input, the conclusion is negative. Al-Attar then addresses an objection about the student's initial belief. He says that if a student begins studying logic believing it has some benefit, but later that belief fades because the subject does not meet his expectation, his effort becomes futile. However, if later he learns of a genuine benefit, his desire is renewed. An objector might say that even if the initial belief fades, the student might later adopt a new belief in the benefit, so the earlier effort is not wasted. Al-Attar responds that this does not negate futility because the word 'becomes' in the author's statement (i.e., 'his effort becomes futile') includes the possibility that it might not become futile if the new belief arises, but if the initial effort is indeed wasted, it shows he lacked insight. Al-Dasuqi then returns to the main explanation, emphasizing that the general knowledge occurs in potentiality close to actuality—not actual knowledge of every issue without reasoning. He clarifies that the syllogism must be explicitly constructed: from the major premise (the definition yields that every logic issue involves the safeguard) and the minor premise (this particular issue involves the safeguard), we conclude it is from logic. He stresses that 'he knows it is from it' means he has the ability (tamakkun) to produce the syllogism and thereby know, not that he knows instantly upon hearing the issue. Al-Attar adds a parallel example: just as a mujtahid has the ability to derive rulings but may still say 'I do not know' for some issues, similarly the ability to classify does not guarantee actual classification in every case. The source of this ability is that the definition of logic is taken from the aspect of the unity (al-wahda) that all issues share, i.e., their common relation to the safeguard against error.

Key Terms
الرسم
العلم الإجمالي
التمكّن
العصمة
المقدمة الكلية

B) A universal premise about every logic issue having input in the safeguard.
C) The definition of logic only.
D) The ability to avoid all error in thinking.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a science provides a universal premise that, when combined with a specific issue's property, yields a categorical conclusion about whether it belongs to that science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition of 'qiyas' (syllogism) as 'a discourse composed of premises that necessitate a conclusion', derive a universal premise about its parts. Then, for a specific example (e.g., a conditional syllogism), construct the categorical syllogism to check if it falls under qiyas. (Hint: The universal premise: 'Every valid syllogism has premises that necessitate a conclusion.')
?
Consider the Islamic science of Fiqh. If the definition of 'riba' (usury) is 'an unlawful excess in exchange', what universal premise does it yield? Use that premise to classify a modern transaction (e.g., a credit sale with interest) as riba or not, by constructing the syllogism.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

Why Logic Studies Words: The Composite Cause
Author explains why the study of words is necessary for conveying and acquiring meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons for studying words in logic and justify why they form a single composite cause rather than separate causes.

Core Idea
Words are the universal medium for teaching and learning logic, making their study indispensable.

In this passage, the author presents the epistemological foundation for including the study of words (alfāẓ) in logic. The matn gives two reasons: the logician’s focus is confined to the concept of the connector (mafhūm al-muwassil), and the communication (ifāda) and acquisition (istifāda) of meanings depend on words. The commentators, al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār, refine these reasons, showing they are not separate but form a single composite cause. Understanding this helps you see why words are a necessary prerequisite for logical study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reasons in the matn
Locate the two reasons: (1) the logician's view is confined to the concept of the connector, and (2) the communication and acquisition of meanings depend on words. Note that 'concept of the connector' means that which leads to unknown conceptual or assential matters.
The matn is the first line before the commentary.
2
Understand the commentators' clarification of 'concept of the connector'
Al-Dasūqī explains that the connector can be conceptual or assential. Al-ʿAṭṭār adds that it refers to the individuals (afrād) of the concept, not the concept itself, to avoid a previous objection.
This shows the precision needed in logical terminology.
3
Analyze the structure of the composite cause
Al-Dasūqī says that the two reasons, along with a third (to come later), are conjuncted as a single cause ('illa wāḥida), not multiple causes. Al-ʿAṭṭār emphasizes that without this composition, the confinement of the logician's view alone would not necessitate the study of words.
The conjunction is grammatical but the logical result is a unified reason.
4
Apply the reasoning to the necessity of studying words
The composite cause shows that words are needed because (a) logic deals with connectors, and (b) those connectors are taught and learned through words. Hence, the study of words is an essential introduction to logic.
This reasoning is not limited to Arabic; it covers all languages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١. لانحصار نَظرِ المنطقي في مفهوم الموصل. وتَوَقُفِ إفادة المعاني واستفادتها على الألفاظ.

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: فِى مَفْهُوْم الْمُوصل الإضافة بيانيَّة؛ أي إلى مجهول تصوري أو تصديقى ؛ كانَ ذلكَ الموصِّلُ تَصوُّريًّا أو تصديقيًّا.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَتَوَقُفِ إِفَادَةِ الْمَعَانِي)؛ أي: الَّتي من جملتها المفهوم الموصل ؛ أي : إفادتها للغير، وقولُهُ: واستفادتها)؛ أي: مِنَ الغير.
وقوله: (وتوقف... إلخ)؛ عطف على انحصار، وَكَذا قوله: (وكونُ الألفاظ)، ومجموع المعطوفين والمعطوف عليه علَّةٌ واحدةٌ لَا عِلَل مُتعدِّدة؛ إِذْ كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ

العطار
مجاز . . . إلخ، المباحث الآتية على أنَّنا لو ارتكبنا هذا التَّأويلَ وصَحَّحنَاهُ؛ رَجَعَتْ أحكام الألفاظ كلّها للدَّلالةِ فَتَأَمَّلْ، لا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ المذكور في هذا الفصل وما بعده تعاريف؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : يؤخذُ مِنْها تلكَ الأحكامُ الَّتي ذكرناها .
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي مَنْهُوْمِ المُوصل ) أي : أفراد مفهوم الموصل؛ لِمَا تقدَّمَ لكَ مِنَ البحث في ذلك، ومَا قِيْلَ: إنَّه لا يُنافي ؛ هذا ما أشار إليهِ سَابِقاً مِنْ أَنَّ المرادَ مِنَ المعلومين الماصدقُ ; لأنَّ المعلومين المذكورينَ هِيَ مَفاهيم أيضاً موصلةٌ ليسَ بشيءٍ; لأنَّ الموصل مفهوم الماصدقات; لا نَفْسُ المفاهيم التي تصدق عليها،
فيلزمُ المحذورُ السَّابق .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَتَوَقُفِ إِفَادَةِ ... إلخ) مِن مُجملةِ التَّعليل ; فَالعَطْفُ مُلاحَظٌ قَبلَهُ، فالعِلَّهُ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ الأمرين، وإلا ; فانحصار نظرِ المنطقي المذكور لا ينتج استحقاق تقدم هذا الفصل، بلْ رُبَّما يوهم عدم الاحتياج لِذِكْرِهِ، وأَمَّا قَوْلُهُ : بعد الفراغِ مِنَ المقدّمة ; فَلَا مدخلَ لَهُ في التَّعليل، وإنَّما هو رجوع للواقع.
(قَوْلُهُ : إِفَادَةِ المَعَانِي واسْتِفَادَتِهَا أي: إفادتها لِلغَيرِ واستفادتها مِنَ الغَيْرِ، قَالَ السَّيِّدُ: مَنْ أراد استفادةَ المنطقِ مِن غيرِهِ أو إفادتَهُ إِيَّاهُ ; احتاج إلى الألفاظ، وكذا في سائر العلوم، فَلِذلكَ عُدَّتْ مَباحِثُ الألفاظ مُقدِّمة الشروع في العُلوم، ثمَّ إِنَّ المنطقي يبحثُ عَنِ الألفاظ على الوجهِ الكُلّيّ المتناول لجميعِ اللُّغَاتِ، فَتَكونُ الحالُ
1. Because the logician's view is confined to the concept of the connector, and because the communication and acquisition of meanings depend on words.

Al-Dasuqi:
(His statement: 'in the concept of the connector' — the idafa is explanatory; i.e., to an unknown conceptual or assential [matter], whether that connector is conceptual or assential.
(His statement: 'and the dependence of the communication of meanings') i.e., which include the concept of the connector; i.e., communicating them to others, and his statement: 'and their acquisition' i.e., from others.
And his statement: ('and dependence... etc.') is conjuncted to 'confinement', and likewise his statement: 'and that words are', and the sum of the conjuncted and that to which they are conjuncted is a single cause, not multiple causes; since each one of them...

Al-Atar:
... metaphor, etc., the following discussions — for if we undertake this interpretation and correct it, all the rulings of words would return to indication, so reflect. It cannot be said that what is mentioned in this chapter and after it are definitions; because we say: those rulings are taken from them.
(His statement: 'in the concept of the connector') i.e., the individuals of the concept of the connector; because of what preceded for you of the investigation in that. And what is said: 'it is not contradictory' — this is what he indicated previously, that what is meant by the two known things is the extension; because the two known mentioned are also concepts that are connectors, [but] that is not valid; because the connector is the concept of the extensions, not the concepts themselves that apply to them, so the previous objection follows.
(His statement: 'and the dependence of the communication... etc.') is part of the overall reasoning; so the conjunction is considered before it, so the cause is composed of the two matters; otherwise, the mentioned confinement of the logician's view would not produce the deservedness of precedence of this chapter, but rather might suggest the lack of need for its mention. As for his statement: 'after finishing the introduction', it has no entry in the reasoning, but is a return to reality.
(His statement: 'the communication of meanings and their acquisition') i.e., communicating them to others and acquiring them from others. Al-Sayyid said: Whoever wants to acquire logic from another or to communicate it to him needs words, and similarly in all sciences. For that reason, the discussions of words were counted as an introduction to the commencement of the sciences. Then indeed the logician investigates words in a universal manner covering all languages, so the situation is.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first two reasons given for studying words in logic. The matn states: 'Because the logician's view is confined to the concept of the connector, and because the communication and acquisition of meanings depend on words.' Al-Dasuqi explains that 'concept of the connector' means that which leads to an unknown tasawwur (concept) or tasdiq (assent), whether the connector itself is conceptual or assential. He clarifies that 'communication of meanings' means teaching them to others, and 'acquisition' means learning from others. He notes that these two reasons, along with a third (to be mentioned later), form a single composite cause, not multiple separate causes. Al-Atar adds that the term 'concept of the connector' refers to the individuals (afrad) of that concept, not the concept itself, to avoid a previous objection. He further explains that the dependence on words applies only to teaching and learning, not to self-acquisition, as one can conceive meanings without words, though the soul is accustomed to associating words with meanings. The passage concludes by stating that the logician studies words in a universal manner applicable to all languages.

إفادة المعاني
استفادة المعاني

Why Conception Precedes Assent
Commentator justifies the order of conception before assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the study of conception (tasawwur) is placed before the study of assent (tasdiq) in Islamic logic texts.

Core Idea
Conception is the foundation; assent is built upon it, not vice versa.

This passage from a classical commentary on logic explains a key pedagogical ordering: why the author begins with conception (tasawwur) before moving to assent (tasdiq). Understanding this ordering reveals the logical priority of forming concepts before making judgments. We will explore the natural priority of conception over assent and its relevance to learning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Recognize that Islamic logic divides knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Conception is mental apprehension without judgment; assent is judgment about truth or falsity.
2
Understand the claim of natural priority
The passage states that 'every conception naturally precedes every assent without the reverse.' Reflect on why: you must conceive the meaning of a term before you can judge a proposition involving it.
This is a logical, not temporal, priority.
3
Apply to a Kalam example
Consider the proposition 'the world is temporally originated.' First you must conceive what 'temporal origination' means, then you can assent to the truth of the proposition.
This shows why tasawwur topics come before tasdiq topics in logic books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. فصل المفهوم: الْمَفْهُوْمُ لَمَّا فرغ من مقدِّماتِ الشُّروعِ في العِلْمِ؛ شرع في المقاصد، وقدَّمَ مَباحث الموصل إلى التَّصورِ على مباحث الموصل إلى التصديقِ؛ لِتقدُّمِ كُلِّ تَصوُّرٍ على كُلِّ تصديق طبعاً من غير عكس، وقدَّمَ فصل الكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتي هي في الأغلب أجزاء للمعرّف على فصله لذلك، أو لنفع معرفتها في معرفته.
Chapter: On the principles of conceptions. The chapter on the concept: After completing the preliminaries of entering into the science, he began the objectives, and he presented the investigations that lead to conception before the investigations that lead to assent, because every conception naturally precedes every assent without the reverse. And he presented the chapter on universals, which are for the most part parts of the definition, before the chapter on definition, either due to the benefit of knowing them in knowing the definition.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note from the commentator (likely al-Dasuqi) justifying the ordering of chapters in the matn. The commentator first identifies that after finishing the introductory matters, the author starts with the main objectives (al-maqasid). He then explains why the topics leading to conception (tasawwur) are placed before those leading to assent (tasdiq): because every conception naturally comes before every assent—this is a natural priority, not a temporal one. He also explains why the chapter on universals (kulliyyat) comes before the chapter on definition (hadd): because universals are usually parts of the definition, so knowing them helps in understanding the definition. This mirrors the pedagogical principle in Islamic logic: one must first grasp simple concepts before forming propositions, and know the building blocks before the construction.

تصديق
كلي
مفهوم

C) Because assent is not needed for knowledge.
D) Because the author arbitrarily decided it.

Key Takeaway
Conception is the building block of assent; knowledge of concepts logically precedes judgment.

Why Universals Precede Definition
Commentator justifies the order of universals before definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the chapter on universals (kulliyyat) is placed before the chapter on definition (hadd) in Islamic logic textbooks.

Core Idea
Universals are the parts; definitions are the whole they compose.

The same passage that explains the priority of conception over assent also explains why universals come before definition. The commentator notes that universals are 'for the most part parts of the definition,' so knowing them aids in understanding definitions. We will analyse this reasoning and its pedagogical value.

Guided Path
1
Recall the nature of definition
A complete definition (hadd) consists of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), both of which are universals. Definitions are built from universals.
2
Extract the commentator's reasoning
The passage states: 'he presented the chapter on universals, which are for the most part parts of the definition, before the chapter on definition, either due to the benefit of knowing them in knowing the definition.' So the order is pedagogically motivated.
The commentator offers two possible reasons (indicated by 'or').
3
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider defining 'salah'. The genus is 'action', the differentia is 'specific actions and recitations' or 'intention'. Knowing the universals 'action' and 'intention' helps construct the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. فصل المفهوم: الْمَفْهُوْمُ لَمَّا فرغ من مقدِّماتِ الشُّروعِ في العِلْمِ؛ شرع في المقاصد، وقدَّمَ مَباحث الموصل إلى التَّصورِ على مباحث الموصل إلى التصديقِ؛ لِتقدُّمِ كُلِّ تَصوُّرٍ على كُلِّ تصديق طبعاً من غير عكس، وقدَّمَ فصل الكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتي هي في الأغلب أجزاء للمعرّف على فصله لذلك، أو لنفع معرفتها في معرفته.
Chapter: On the principles of conceptions. The chapter on the concept: After completing the preliminaries of entering into the science, he began the objectives, and he presented the investigations that lead to conception before the investigations that lead to assent, because every conception naturally precedes every assent without the reverse. And he presented the chapter on universals, which are for the most part parts of the definition, before the chapter on definition, either due to the benefit of knowing them in knowing the definition.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note from the commentator (likely al-Dasuqi) justifying the ordering of chapters in the matn. The commentator first identifies that after finishing the introductory matters, the author starts with the main objectives (al-maqasid). He then explains why the topics leading to conception (tasawwur) are placed before those leading to assent (tasdiq): because every conception naturally comes before every assent—this is a natural priority, not a temporal one. He also explains why the chapter on universals (kulliyyat) comes before the chapter on definition (hadd): because universals are usually parts of the definition, so knowing them helps in understanding the definition. This mirrors the pedagogical principle in Islamic logic: one must first grasp simple concepts before forming propositions, and know the building blocks before the construction.

Why did the author place the chapter on universals before the chapter on definition, according to the passage?
A) Because universals are easier to memorize.
B) Because universals are often parts of the definition.
C) Because definitions are not needed without universals.
D) Because the author followed a traditional sequence.

Key Takeaway
Universals provide the building blocks for constructing precise definitions; learning them first is pedagogically sound.

The Purpose of Logic and the Nature of Thought
Author explains the purpose of logic and introduces the two aspects: tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the purpose of logic as the evaluation of thought and define 'thought' (al-fikr) as a rational process of arranging known matters to reach an unknown.

Core Idea
Logic is not about learning arguments — it is about knowing which thought is sound and which is corrupt.

This opening passage establishes why logic matters: it is the tool to distinguish correct from incorrect reasoning. The author grounds logic in the process of thought (fikr), defined as ordering known matters to reach unknown ones. Understanding this purpose is crucial for all subsequent logical study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated purpose of logic
Find the sentence that begins with 'الْغَرَضُ مِنَ الْمَنْطِقِ'. What is the purpose according to the author?
2
Define 'thought' (al-fikr) from the commentary
Read al-Dasuqi's explanation: 'الْفِكْرُ أَيْ تَرْتِيبُ أُمُورٍ مَعْلُومَةٍ لِلتَّوَصُّلِ إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ'. What are the key components?
The definition involves both known matters and an unknown target.
3
Differentiate correctness from corruption
How does the commentary define 'sihha' and 'fasad'? Correctness is when thought gathers the conditions; corruption when it misses them.
4
Connect to the two domains of unknowns
The passage says thought aims at two types of unknowns: conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertoric (tasdiqi). Identify these terms in the text.
This division will be developed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المُعَرِّفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ مِنَ الْمَنْطِقِ مَعْرِفَةُ صِحَّةِ الْفِكْرِ وَفَسَادِهِ. وَالْفِكْرُ إِمَّا لِتَحْصِيلِ الْمَجْهُولَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ أَوِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ، فَيَكُونُ لِلْمَنْطِقِ طَرَفَانِ: تَصَوُّرَاتٌ وَتَصْدِيقَاتٌ، وَلِكُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا مَبَادِئُ وَمَقَاصِدُ.
وَقَالَ الدُّسُوقِيُّ: (قَوْلُهُ: أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ) أَيِ الْمَقْصُودُ. (قَوْلُهُ: الْفِكْرُ) أَيْ تَرْتِيبُ أُمُورٍ مَعْلُومَةٍ لِلتَّوَصُّلِ إِلَى مَجْهُولٍ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَصِحَّتُهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ اسْتِجْمَاعِهِ الشُّرُوطَ، وَفَسَادُهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ عَدَمِ اسْتِجْمَاعِهَا. (قَوْلُهُ: طَرَفَانِ) أَيْ مُجْزِآنِ. (قَوْلُهُ: تَصَوُّرَاتٌ) أَيْ مَا أَفَادَ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ مِنَ الْقَوْلِ الشَّارِحِ، وَالتَّصَوُّرُ إِدْرَاكُ الْمُفْرَدِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَتَصْدِيقَاتٌ) أَيْ مَا أَفَادَهَا مِنَ الْحُجَجِ، وَالتَّصْدِيقُ إِدْرَاكُ النِّسْبَةِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلِكُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا) أَيْ مِنَ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ الْمَجْهُولَةِ وَالتَّصْدِيقَاتِ الْمَجْهُولَةِ. (قَوْلُهُ: وَمَقَاصِدُ) أَيْ مُفِيدٌ لِتِلْكَ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ وَالتَّصْدِيقَاتِ.
وَقَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: (قَوْلُهُ: اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ ... إِلَخ) هَذَا تَمْهِيدٌ لِقَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: مَعْرِفَةُ الشَّيْءِ ... إِلَخ. (قَوْلُهُ: فَيَكُونُ لِلْمَنْطِقِ طَرَفَانِ) أَيْ قِسْمَانِ، وَفِي نُسْخَةٍ: طَرِيقَانِ، فَيُرَادُ مِنَ الْمَنْطِقِ حِينَئِذٍ مَقَاصِدُ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ وَمَقَاصِدُ التَّصْدِيقَاتِ، وَسَقَطَ مَا تَكَلَّفَ بِهِ الْبَعْضُ هُنَا.
Chapter: On the definiens and its divisions. Know that the purpose of logic is to know the correctness of thought and its corruption. Thought is either for acquiring unknown concepts or unknown assents. So for logic there are two sides: concepts and assents. For each of them there are principles and aims. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'that the purpose' means the intention. His statement: 'thought' means arranging known matters to arrive at an unknown. Then its correctness is an expression of its gathering the conditions, and its corruption is an expression of its not gathering them. His statement: 'two sides' means two parts. His statement: 'concepts' means what provides concepts from the explicatory statement, and concept is apprehension of the simple. His statement: 'and assents' means what provides them from the proofs, and assent is apprehension of the relation. His statement: 'and for each of them' means from the unknown concepts and unknown assents. His statement: 'and aims' means what is useful for those concepts and assents. Al-Attar said: His statement: 'Know that the purpose... etc.' This is a preface to the author's statement: 'definition of the thing... etc.' His statement: 'so for logic there are two sides' means two divisions, and in a copy: two paths. Then by logic is meant here the aims of concepts and the aims of assents, and what some labored over here is dropped.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage opens the section on the definiens (المعرف) with a preface (تمهيد) that establishes the epistemological foundation of logic. The author states that the purpose of logic is to distinguish correct thought from incorrect thought. Thought is directed at two types of unknowns: conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertoric (tasdiqi). Therefore, logic deals with two corresponding aspects: concepts and assents, each having principles (mabadi') and aims (maqasid). The commentators expand on these terms. Al-Dasuqi defines 'thought' as the arrangement of known matters to reach an unknown; correctness means fulfilling the necessary conditions, corruption means failing to do so. He explains that 'concepts' are provided by the explicatory statement (qawl sharh) and are the apprehension of a simple term, while 'assents' are provided by proofs (hujaj) and are the apprehension of a relation. Al-Attar clarifies that this entire passage is a preface to the later definition of 'definiens', and he notes a variant reading 'two paths' instead of 'two sides', indicating that logic here refers to the aims of concepts and assents. The passage sets the stage for the subsequent discussion of definitions and proof.

A) Definitions and proofs

D) Correctness and corruption

Key Takeaway
Logic aims at discriminating correct from corrupt thought, and thought itself is the ordered movement from knowns to an unknown.

The Two Aspects of Logic: Concepts and Assents
Author explains the purpose of logic and introduces the two aspects: tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish concepts (tasawwur) from assents (tasdiq) and explain their respective principles and aims.

Core Idea
Logic has two sides: simple concepts and propositional assents — each requiring its own tools.

Once the purpose of logic is established, the author introduces the two fundamental divisions: concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). The commentators clarify that concepts are grasped through explanatory statements (qawl sharh) and assents through proofs (hujaj). Each division has its own starting points (mabadi') and goals (maqasid). This foundational classification organizes the entire science of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides (tarafan) in the text
Find the sentence that says 'فَيَكُونُ لِلْمَنْطِقِ طَرَفَانِ' and list them.
The two sides are concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat).
2
Distinguish the definitions from the commentaries
Al-Dasuqi defines tasawwur as 'إِدْرَاكُ الْمُفْرَدِ' (apprehension of the simple) and tasdiq as 'إِدْرَاكُ النِّسْبَةِ' (apprehension of the relation). Compare these two.
Tasawwur is a simple grasp; tasdiq involves a judgment about a relation.
3
Identify the principles (mabadi') and aims (maqasid) for each
The passage states that each of the two has principles and aims. From the commentary, the principles for concepts are the five universals, and for assents they are premises. The aims are definitions for concepts and syllogisms for assents.
This will be elaborated in later sections.
4
Reflect on the variant reading (tarifan vs. tariqan)
Al-Attar mentions a variant 'two paths'. Consider how this wording emphasizes the methodological function of logic.

Introduction and Front Matter

8 min
The Second Purpose: Transition from Conceptions to Assents
Commentator defines 'al-maqsad' and explains the transition to assents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical distinction between tasawwurat and tasdiqat and analyse the commentator's resolution of the apparent self-containment problem in the phrase 'al-maqsad al-thani fi al-tasdiqat'.

Core Idea
The second maqsad is not a place but a collection of topics, distinguished from tasawwurat by a general-detailed relationship.

This passage marks a major transition in the study of logic: from conceptions (tasawwurat) to assents (tasdiqat). The commentator clarifies what 'al-maqsad' means in this context and addresses a subtle problem—how can assents be both the purpose and the topic? Understanding this distinction is essential for navigating the rest of the logical work and for seeing how the two parts of logic relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transition
Find the sentence that signals the shift from tasawwurat to tasdiqat. Note that the author mentions 'completion of the discussions of conceptions' before starting the second purpose.
This transitional formula is conventional in logical works.
2
Parse the Term 'al-Maqsad'
The commentator defines 'al-maqsad' as 'place of intention' (maqam al-qasd) but clarifies it here means 'the topics related to the states of assents'. Understand that the term is used metaphorically.
Contrast this with the literal meaning of 'intention' (qasd) to see the nuance.
3
Extract the Objection
The commentator anticipates an objection: if tasdiqat are themselves the topics investigating tasdiq, then saying 'the second purpose on assents' would imply a thing contained in itself. Locate this objection in the text.
The objection is introduced with 'la yuqalu' (it should not be said).
4
Understand the Commentator's Resolution
The commentator offers two ways to resolve the objection: either the 'maqsad' is general (mujmal) while the topics are detailed (mufassal)—sufficient distinction—or 'maqsad' is taken literally as 'intention' (qasd), avoiding the problem entirely.
The second interpretation is simpler and does not require a conceptual distinction.
5
Recognize the Pedagogical Purpose
The commentator notes that adducing this transitional statement energizes the student and highlights the distinction between the two sections. Understand that this is a standard pedagogical technique.
Look for similar transitional statements in other sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
[تمهيد]
[المقْصَدُ الثَّانِي: فِي التَّصْدِيْقَاتِ]
ولَمَّا وقعَ الفراغ من مباحثِ التَّصوراتِ مباديها ومقاصدِها؛ شَرَعَ في التصديقات، ولها أيضاً مبادي ومقاصد.
الدسوقي
فصل: في التصديقات
(قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ مَبَاحِثِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ أي من القضايا التي يبحث فيها عن التَّصورات ، وقوله : (مباديها ومقاصدها)؛ عطف تفسير للتصورات .
العطار
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ
الحمدُ للهِ ربِّ العالمين، وصلَّى الله على سيّدِنَا مُحمَّدٍ وآلِهِ وصحبه أجمعين، وهذا ما وعدْنَا بِهِ مِنَ الكلام على التصديقاتِ فَنقولُ وَبِاللهِ التَّوفيقُ : (قَوْلُهُ : المَقْصَدُ الثَّانِي فِي التَّصْدِيقَاتِ) المقصد : مكان القصد، والمراد به هنا : المسائل المتعلّقة بأحوالِ التصديقات؛ لأنَّ قصد المصنفِ تَعَلَّقَ بجمعها بعد فراغِهِ من مباحثِ التَّصوراتِ، لا يُقالُ : التصديقاتُ هِي المسائل الباحثة عن أحوالِ التصديقِ كَمَا اعترفت به، فيلزمُ ظرفيَّةُ الشَّيء في نفسِهِ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : ما تعلَّقَ القصد؛ مُجمل، وهذهِ مُفصَّلةٌ، وهذا القدر كافٍ في المغايرة، أو يُرادُ مِنَ المقصدِ القصد؛ فلا يحتاج لدعوى المغايرة الاعتبارية في تصحيح الظَّرفيَّةِ، فَإِنْ جعل المصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول ؛ أي: المقصود؛ رجع إلى الاعتبارِ الأَوَّلِ، فَيحتاج لدعوى المغايرة الاعتباريَّة في تصحيح الظرفية، إلَّا أَنَّهُ على الأَوَّلِ تُعتبرُ المسائل محلا للقصد، وفي هذا الاعتبار يُعتبرُ القصد واقعاً عليها . (قَوْلُهُ: وَلَمَّا وَقَعَ الْفَرَاعُ ... إِلَخ) جرَتْ عادةُ الشَّارحين بإيراد هذه القضيَّةِ الاتفاقية بعد الفراغ من مبحث والشُّروعِ في آخر؛ تنشيطاً للمتعلم فيما سيأتي، حيث حصَّلَ قدراً مُعتداً بِهِ مِنَ العِلْم، وتنبيهاً على المغايرة بين المبحثين، بحيث إذا وقعَتْ مسألةٌ مِمَّا تقدَّمَ فيما تأخَّرَ؛ يكون ذلكَ بِطَريق الاستطراد أو المبدئية.
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions [Preamble] [The Second Purpose: On Assents] And when the completion of the discussions of conceptions, their principles and purposes, occurred, he began on assents, and they also have principles and purposes. al-Dasuqi: Chapter: On Assents (His saying: 'from the discussions of conceptions' means from the propositions in which conceptions are investigated, and his saying: 'their principles and purposes' is an explanatory apposition to conceptions.) al-Attar: In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and may God bless our master Muhammad and his family and companions altogether. This is what we promised of discourse on assents. So we say, and with God is success: (His saying: 'The Second Purpose: On Assents') Al-Maqsad: the place of intention, and what is meant here is: the topics related to the states of assents, because the intention of the author was directed to collecting them after his completion of the discussions of conceptions. It should not be said: 'The assents are themselves the topics that investigate the states of assent, as you have acknowledged, so it would necessitate the containment of a thing in itself.' For we say: What is intended by 'purpose' is general, and these [topics] are detailed, and this is sufficient for distinction. Or what is meant by al-maqsad is 'intention' (al-qasd), then there is no need to claim a conceptual distinction to correct the containment. For if the verbal noun is taken in the sense of the passive participle, i.e., the intended thing, it returns to the first consideration, so one needs a conceptual distinction to correct the containment. However, on the first [interpretation], the topics are considered a locus for the intention, and on this consideration, the intention is considered as occurring upon them. (His saying: 'And when the completion... etc.') The custom of commentators is to adduce this transitional statement after completing one topic and beginning another, to energize the student for what is coming, since he has acquired a considerable amount of knowledge, and as a notification of the distinction between the two topics, so that if a topic from what has preceded occurs in what comes later, it is by way of digression or preliminary.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the second major section of logic: tasdiqat (assents). The commentator explains the transition: after completing the discussion of tasawwurat (conceptions) including their principles and objectives, the author now turns to tasdiqat, which also have their own principles and objectives. The commentator then defines the term 'al-maqsad' (the purpose) as 'the place of intention' (maqam al-qasd), but here it refers to the topics related to the states of tasdiqat. He addresses a potential objection: if tasdiqat are themselves the topics that investigate tasdiq, then saying 'al-maqsad al-thani fi al-tasdiqat' would imply something being contained in itself. He responds by saying that what the author intended is general (mujmal) while the details are particular (mufassal), which suffices for distinction. Alternatively, 'al-maqsad' could be taken literally as 'intention' (al-qasd) itself, avoiding the need for a conceptual distinction. Then the commentator notes that it is customary for commentators to mention this transitional statement between sections to motivate the student and highlight the difference between the two topics, ensuring that any overlap is understood as digression or preliminary material.

Key Terms
المقصد
التصديقات
التصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Attar's commentary, how is the objection 'containment of a thing in itself' resolved?
A) By redefining 'tasdiqat' to mean something else.
B) By distinguishing between a general purpose and detailed topics, or by taking 'maqsad' as intention.
C) By deleting the phrase 'fi al-tasdiqat' from the title.
D) By arguing that the author made a grammatical mistake.

Key Takeaway
The transition from tasawwurat to tasdiqat in logic is not just a change of topic, but a shift from defining terms to judging propositions, and understanding the terminology 'al-maqsad' is key to avoiding conceptual confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction and Front Matter
