Introduction to Tasdiqat: Scope and Principles
Commentators introduce the second section on tasdiqat, defining its scope and structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the principles of tasdiqat from those of tasawwurat and explain why only certain propositions are used in logical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Tasdiqat builds on tasawwurat, but its principles are propositions—only those that compose syllogisms matter.

In the first section, we covered concepts (tasawwurat) and their principles. Now the author turns to assertions (tasdiqat), which also have their own principles and goals. The primary principle of this section is propositions (qadaya) and their divisions. However, not all propositions are useful for logical proof; only those that can serve as premises in a syllogism are relevant. This lesson unpacks this transition and the key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural transition
Notice that after completing tasawwurat, the author begins tasdiqat. This mirrors the two main parts of logic.
The text says: 'ولما وقع الفراغ من مباحث التصورات... شرع في التصديقات'
2
Locate the principles of tasdiqat
Find the phrase 'فمباديها: القضايا وأقسامها' – the principles are propositions and their divisions.
3
Understand the qualification from al-Isam
Al-Isam clarifies that the proof does not depend on all propositions, only those that compose syllogisms; natural science propositions (tabi'iyyat) are excluded.
This parallels how definition does not use all universals.
4
Analyze the species-in-definition nuance
Al-Isam discusses whether species can be part of a definiens. In nominal definitions of conventional entities (e.g., 'Rumi'), species can function as a genus.
The ruling that species is not part may be an oversight or specific to real essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني: في التصديقات. ولما وقع الفراغ من مباحث التصورات مباديها ومقاصدها؛ شرع في التصديقات، ولها أيضاً مباد ومقاصد. فمباديها: القضايا وأقسامها. قال العصام في حاشية القطب: وههنا بحث شريف، وهو أن توقف الحجة ليس على جميع القضايا؛ بل يتوقف على قضايا يتركب منها، فإن الطبيعيات لا تنفع في الأقيسة، كما أن التعريف لم يتوقف على جميع الكليات؛ بل على ما سوى النوع والعرض العام أيضاً عند المتأخرين؛ إذ لا يتركب منهما معرَّف، فذكر الطبيعية ههنا لمزيد تحقيق القضايا المهمة، كما أن بيان النوع والعرض العام لمزيد تحقيق الكليات المهمة، ومنهم من قال: يتركب المعرَّف من النوع أيضاً، كما يقال في تعريف الصنف: الرومي إنسان من بلاد الروم، فحكم القوم بأن النوع لا يكون جزءاً من التعريف؛ إما سهو، وإما مختص بما سوى الماهيات الاعتبارية، وليس لأن تعريف الرومي تعريف اسمي، والنوع يصح أن يكون تمام المشترك بين مفهومين اسميين، فيكون بهذا الاعتبار جنساً، فتعريف الرومي؛ تعريف الشيء بجنسه لا بنوعه. ثم إن تعريف القضايا لا بد من تقديمه لتصور موضوعات المسائل ومحمولاتها الآتية، وأما تقسيمها إلى الأقسام الأولية؛ فإنه كالتتمة للتعريف؛ لأن به يتم ما هو الغرض من التعريف؛ أعني: الانكشاف التام والتعيين.
The Second Objective: On Assertions. And when the discussion of concepts, their principles and goals, was completed, he began on assertions, and they also have principles and goals. Their principles: propositions and their divisions. Al-Isam said in the gloss of al-Qutb: Here is a noble investigation, which is that the dependence of the proof is not on all propositions; rather, it depends on propositions from which the syllogism is composed, for natural matters are not useful in syllogisms, just as the definition does not depend on all universals; rather, on other than species and accidental universal also according to the later scholars; since a definiens is not composed of them. So mentioning natural matters here is for greater verification of important propositions, just as the explanation of species and accidental universal is for greater verification of important universals. And some said: The definiens is composed of species as well, as is said in defining a type: 'Rumi is a man from the land of Rum.' So the people's ruling that species is not a part of the definition is either an oversight or is specific to other than conventional essences, and it is not because the definition of 'Rumi' is a nominal definition, and species can be the complete commonality between two nominal concepts, thus in this respect it is a genus. So the definition of 'Rumi' is a definition of a thing by its genus, not by its species. Then the definition of propositions must be presented for conceiving the subjects and predicates of the coming issues. And as for their division into primary divisions, it is like a completion of the definition, because by it the purpose of the definition is achieved, namely complete disclosure and specification.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the second main section of the work, focusing on tasdiqat (assertions or judgments). It begins by stating that after finishing the discussions on tasawwurat (concepts) including their principles and goals, the author now turns to tasdiqat, noting that they too have principles and goals. The first principle is propositions (qadaya) and their divisions. The commentator then quotes al-Isam (from his gloss on al-Qutb) raising a subtle investigation: the dependence of a proof (hujja) is not on all propositions, but only on those from which syllogisms are composed. For example, natural science propositions are not used in logical syllogisms. Similarly, definition does not require all universals; later scholars exclude species and accidental universal because a definiens cannot be formed from them alone. However, al-Isam notes an exception: in nominal definitions of conventional entities (like 'Rumi' meaning a person from Rum), species can function as a genus because it combines two nominal concepts. He clarifies that the ruling that species cannot be a part of definition is either an oversight or applies only to real essences, not nominal ones. Finally, the passage underscores that defining propositions is necessary to understand the subjects and predicates of later discussions, and dividing them into primary divisions completes the definition by achieving full clarity and specification.

مبادئ
مقاصد
قضية
طبيعيات

C) The syllogism and its figures.
D) The five universals.

Key Takeaway
The principles of tasdiqat are propositions, but only those that can compose syllogisms are relevant; natural science propositions are excluded.

?

Why Propositions Come First in Logic
Author explains why propositions are presented first in logic texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for presenting propositions before syllogism and proof in logic texts, using the concepts of dependency (tawaqquf) and primary vs. secondary divisions.

Core Idea
Propositions are principles; syllogisms depend on them.

This passage from the commentary explains a key methodological choice in logic textbooks: why start with propositions (qadaya) before moving to syllogism (qiyas) and proof (hujja)? The commentator al-‘Attar clarifies that the order is not arbitrary but based on the dependency of the aims (maqasid) on the principles (mabadi'). Understanding this rationale helps you appreciate the structure of any logical work and gives you a tool for evaluating topic order in other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms: mabadi’ and maqasid
Find in the Arabic text where the commentator states that the aims (maqasid) are syllogism and proof, and that they depend on the principles (mabadi’). What are the principles in this context?
Principles here refer to propositions (qadaya) and their divisions and rulings.
2
Understand the dependency (tawaqquf)
The text says: 'It is necessary to present the principles because the aims depend on them.' Explain why syllogism and proof cannot be understood without first understanding propositions.
Syllogisms are composed of propositions; their validity relies on propositional properties like conversion and contradiction.
3
Follow the objection and the responses
An objection is raised: the dependency argument only proves that principles in general must come first, not that propositions specifically must precede other topics. How do the commentators answer? There are two answers given. Summarise each.
The first answer: the explanation is about the reality of presenting principles; order among them is conventional. The second: the definition of a proposition is itself a principle for its rulings.
4
Distinguish primary vs. secondary division
The commentator explains that dividing propositions into categorical and conditional is a primary division that reveals the essence of a proposition. Secondary divisions (like dividing categorical into affirmative and negative) only increase understanding of the section. Why does the primary division matter more?
Primary divisions disclose the thing itself; secondary divisions only clarify subcategories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في المعرَّفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ وأحكامها ومقاصدها : القياس والحجة. ولا بُدَّ من تقديم المبادي؛ لتوقف المقاصدِ عليها؛ فَلِهذا قدَّم القضايا، وقال في تعريفها : الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَحْكَامهَا كَالعكس والتَّناقض وتلازم الشَّرطيات، وإنْ كانَ المصنِّفُ لم يذكر هذا الثَّالث، أو أنَّ المراد بالجمع : ما فوقَ الواحد. منه (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَوَقُفِ الْمَقَاصِدِ ... إلخ) أي : توقف الكلِّ على جزئه المركَّبِ (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيْفِهَا أي: في تعريف مفردِهَا، وهو القضيَّة؛ لأنَّ التَّعريف إنَّما هو للحقائق لا للأفراد . العطار هي، الأقسامِ الأوَّليَّةِ الَّتي تحصيلها فرغ تحصيل المقسم، فتنكشفُ القضايا بذلك مزيد انكشاف، وأمَّا التَّقسيمُ الثَّانوي؛ فإنَّما يوجبُ زيادةَ انكشافِ القسم، فإِنَّ تَقسيم القضيَّةِ الحمليّة يوجب مزيد انكشافِهَا؛ لا مزيدَ انكشاف القضيَّةِ مِن حيث والذي يُوجب مزيد انكشافها من حيثُ هِي؛ تَقسيمها إلى الحمليةِ والشَّرطية. (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَحْكَامُهَا ) أي : بيانُ أحكامِهَا مِنَ التَّناقض والعكوس. (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَالَ فِي تَعْرِيْفِهَا الظَّاهِرُ عطفه على قسم، فيكونُ مِن جملة المعلَّلِ بِمَا إليه بذا، والمعنى حينئذٍ تقديم المبادي واجب لتوقف المقاصدِ عليها، ولأجل ذلكَ؛ قدَّمَ القضايا وقال في تعريفها : فيردُ أنَّ التَّعليلَ إِنَّما ينتجُ وجوب تقديم المبادي مطلقاً؛ لا تقديم خصوص القضايا، وقد يُجابُ بأنَّ المقصود مِنَ التَّعليل : بيان حقيقة تقديم المبادي، وأمَّا تقديم بعضها على بعض؛ فَأمره شهير، إذ الحكم على الشَّيء فرع عن تصوُّرِهِ، والمفيد للتَّصورِ التَّعريفُ، والتَّقسيمُ مِن تتمَّتِهِ كَمَا سمعْتَ، فوجبَ التَّرتيب بين هذهِ المباحثِ؛ فقدَّمَ القضايا، أو يُقالُ : إِنَّ تعريف القضيَّةِ مبدأ بالنّسبة لأحكامِهَا، وقد أفادَ التَّعليل ذلك.
Section: On the known [proposition], its divisions, its rulings, and its aims: syllogism and proof. It is necessary to present the principles because the aims depend on them; therefore, the author presented the propositions, and said in defining them: Dusooqi: His statement: 'and their rulings such as conversion, contradiction, and the implication of conditionals, even if the author did not mention this third, or that the plural means more than one.' From him: His statement: 'because the aims depend... etc.' means: the whole depends on its compound part. His statement: 'and said in defining them' means in defining their singular, which is the proposition, because definition is of realities, not individuals. Al-‘Attar: They are the primary divisions whose attainment is the attainment of the thing divided, so the propositions become more disclosed by that. As for secondary division, it only causes increased disclosure of the section. For the division of the categorical proposition causes increased disclosure of it, not increased disclosure of the proposition as such. What causes increased disclosure of it as such is its division into categorical and conditional. His statement: 'and their rulings' means the explanation of their rulings from contradiction and conversion. His statement: 'and said in defining them': apparently it is coordinated with 'section', so it is part of what is explained by the preceding. The meaning then is that presenting the principles is obligatory because the aims depend on them, and for that reason he presented the propositions and said in defining them. But it is objected that the explanation only yields the obligation of presenting principles absolutely, not the particular presentation of propositions. It may be answered that the purpose of the explanation is to clarify the reality of presenting principles; as for presenting some over others, it is well-known. For judgment about a thing is a branch of its conception, and what is useful for conception is definition, and division is a completion of it as you have heard, so ordering among these topics is necessary; thus he presented the propositions. Or it is said: The definition of the proposition is a principle in relation to its rulings, and the explanation has indicated that.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a methodological section from the commentary on a logic text. It explains why the author of the matn (the original text) presented the topic of propositions (qadaya) before other topics like syllogism and proof. The commentator, al-‘Attar, explains that the principles (mabadi') must be introduced first because the aims (maqasid) depend on them. The aims of logic are syllogism and proof, and propositions are the means by which these are constructed. The author first discusses the definition of the proposition, its divisions, and its rulings (such as conversion and contradiction). The passage then addresses an objection: the explanation that 'the aims depend on the principles' only proves that principles must be presented in general, not that propositions in particular must come first. The commentator offers two answers: first, the explanation is about the reality of presenting principles, but the order among them is conventional; second, the definition of the proposition is itself a principle for its rulings, so it must come first. The passage also clarifies that definition is of realities, not of individuals, and that primary divisions (like dividing proposition into categorical and conditional) increase understanding of the thing itself, while secondary divisions (like dividing categorical into affirmative and negative) only increase understanding of the section.

Key Takeaway
The order of topics in logic is determined by dependency: principles must precede aims, and primary divisions must precede secondary divisions.

Consider the structure of a typical textbook on Usul al-Fiqh. Identify which topics are 'mabadi' and which are 'maqasid'. Explain why the author might have chosen that order using the logic of tawaqquf.

The Purpose of Examples in Islamic Logic
Methodological note on the purpose of examples in logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why examples in classical logic texts need not be factually true and how commentators elucidate the author's pedagogical intent.

Core Idea
Examples illustrate rules; they need not correspond to reality.

In this lesson, we examine a brief but crucial methodological note from the main sharh: the purpose of examples in logic is purely illustrative, not factual. The commentaries by al-Dasooqi and al-Attar unpack this point, revealing a sophisticated understanding of pedagogy. You will learn to recognize the function of hypothetical examples and the metaphor of 'closing the eyes' to any discrepancy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the sharh's statement: 'the purpose of presenting examples is to clarify the rules, whether they correspond to reality or not.' Note the key phrase 'by mere supposition' (بِمُجَرَّدِ الْفَرْضِ).
This is the core pedagogical principle.
2
Understand the metaphor
Al-Sharnubi, quoted by al-Attar, explains that the sharh uses a metaphor: the article about the example is likened to a speck in the eye; one should 'close the eyes' to its ugliness (i.e., lack of factual match). This shows the example's purpose is not factual but illustrative.
The metaphor is common in Arabic rhetorical tradition.
3
Trace the commentary chain
Al-Dasooqi comments on three phrases: (1) 'by mere supposition' — confirming the spelling; (2) 'if they are not corresponding to reality' — he doubts this, as he believes all examples in the book are factual; (3) 'draw the hem of closing the eyes' — explaining the grammar. Al-Attar then interprets the metaphor and suggests al-Dasooqi's 'ponder' refers to examples from other parts of the book, not just this chapter.
Notice how later commentators refine and expand the earlier interpretation.
4
Apply the principle
When reading a logic text, consider whether an example is factual or hypothetical. If it seems unrealistic, do not reject the rule it illustrates. The example is a tool for learning the logical form.
This prevents confusion when encountering seemingly false premises in examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمْ : أنَّ المقصود من إيرادِ الأمثلة إيضاح القواعدِ؛ سواء طابقت الواقع أو لا، فإنَّ التَّمثيل يحصل بمجرد الفرض، فالأمثلة التي أوردناها ، إنْ كانَت غيرَ مُطابقةٍ لِلواقع؛ فعليك أن تسحب ذيل الإغماض عن المقالِ ؛ إذْ لا مناقشة في المثالِ .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: بِمُجَرَّدِ الْفَرْضِ) بالفاء .
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ مُطَابِقَةٍ لِلْوَاقِعِ) تأمَّل (١) ، فإِنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أَنَّ الأمثلةَ كُلَّها مطابقة للواقع .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنْ تَشحَبَ ذَيْلَ الْإِغْمَاضٍ عَنِ الْمَقَالِ) مُتعَلَّقٌ (۲) بِالإعْماضِ.
العطار
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : تأمل ... إلخ) لعله يريد أمثلة الكتاب لا خصوص ما في هذا الفصل. (٢) (قَوْلُهُ : متعلق . إلخ) الظاهر أن كلام الشارح استعارة بالكناية حيث شبه المقال في المثال بالقذا في العين بجامع القبح في كل وحذف المشبه به ورمز إليه بذكر لازمه، وهو الإغماض وإثبات الإغماض للمقال تخييلاً. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Know that the purpose of presenting examples is to clarify the rules, whether they correspond to reality or not, for illustration occurs by mere supposition. So the examples we have presented, if they do not correspond to reality, you should draw the hem of closing the eyes over the article, for there is no debate about the example. Al-Dasooqi: His saying: 'by mere supposition' is with fa'. His saying: 'if they are not corresponding to reality' — ponder (1), for the apparent is that all examples correspond to reality. His saying: 'you draw the hem of closing the eyes over the article' — the attachment (2) is to 'closing the eyes'. Al-Attar: (1) His saying: 'ponder ... etc.' Perhaps he intends the examples of the book, not specifically those in this chapter. (2) His saying: 'attachment ... etc.' The apparent is that the commentator's speech is a metaphor by metonymy, likening the article about the example to a speck in the eye, with the commonality of ugliness, and he omitted the likened and alluded to it by mentioning its necessary implication, which is closing the eyes, and affirming closing the eyes for the article by imagination. End quote from al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 904

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note (تنبيه هام) from the main sharh, stating that examples in logic are for illustration only and need not be factually correct. Al-Dasooqi comments on three phrases: 'by mere supposition' confirms the letter fa' in the word 'al-fard'. On the statement 'if they are not corresponding to reality', he says to ponder, because in his view all examples in the book are factually accurate. On the phrase 'draw the hem of closing the eyes', he explains that it is an attachment to 'closing the eyes'. Al-Attar then expands on the metaphor: the sharh likens the example to a speck in the eye, suggesting that one should overlook any lack of correspondence. This shows that the purpose of examples is purely pedagogical.

التمثيل
الإغماض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the note, what is the purpose of examples in logic?
A) To provide factual evidence for claims.
B) To clarify the rules, even if they do not match reality.
C) To test the reader's knowledge of real-world facts.
D) To make the text longer.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, examples are pedagogical tools that illustrate rules; they need not be factually accurate, and readers should overlook any discrepancy to focus on the logical lesson.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in a logic text (e.g., 'Every A is B, some C is A, therefore some C is B'). Explain whether the truth of the premises matters for the validity of the argument. Then state the purpose of giving such an example.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Scholarly Humility of a Teacher: Seeking Forgiveness and Correction
Author concludes his work with apologies and acknowledgments of limited sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rhetorical and ethical conventions used by a classical scholar to conclude a logical textbook.

Core Idea
Sincere scholarship requires acknowledging one's limitations and inviting correction from peers.

The concluding section of a scholarly work is not merely a formality. It is a window into the author's character and his relationship with knowledge. Here, the author of this logic textbook humbly distances himself from those he considers true authors, confesses his limited access to sources, and explicitly asks readers to correct his errors. This lesson will help you recognize the conventions of humility and the ethical posture expected of a scholar in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's self-deprecation
Identify phrases where the author contrasts himself with 'those who deserved the rank of authorship'. Note the metaphor of the 'Anqa bird to emphasize the vast difference.
The 'Anqa symbolizes something unattainable.
2
Identify the request for correction
Find how the author asks his peers and friends to handle his mistakes. He asks them to 'cover it with correction' (فليستروه بالتصحيح). This means writing corrections in the margin, not erasing the original.
Al-Dassuqi explains this method prevents accidental removal of correct content.
3
Analyze the confession of limited sources
The author states he only had access to one commentary (on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya). He extracted issues according to his own intellect (ذهني ودهائي). Note the humility in attributing his work to his own limited ability.
This does not preclude using other sources; al-Dassuqi notes he also used al-Sayyid's Hashiya occasionally.
4
Understand the theological humility
The author seeks forgiveness 'from my forgetfulness'. Al-Dassuqi clarifies that forgetfulness itself is not a sin; the request is for the underlying shortcoming that caused it.
This reflects the Islamic concept that human effort is fallible and requires divine mercy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاتمة
الذين استحقوا مرتبةَ التَّصنيف ولا ممَّن كان بالحقِّ بمثالِ منقبة التأليف، ومع ذلك لو وقع تصنيفي هذا عند الحضرة الخاقانية في حيّز القَبُولِ ؛ لاشتهر في الأقطار إشهار الصَّبَا والقَبُول .
ثمَّ المأمولُ من مكارم الأقرانِ ومحاسن الخلان أن يتجاوزوا عما فيه مِن السَّهو والنسيان بالصَّفح والغفران، وإن عثروا على الخطأ الصريح؛

جزى الله خيراً من تأمَّلَ صنعتي وقابلَ ما فيها من الشهو بالعفو وأصلح ما أخطأت فيه بفضلِهِ وفطنته وأسـتـغـفـر الله مـن سـهـوي فإنّي معترفُ بقلَّةِ البضاعة ورجلي في مضمار تلك الصناعة؛ إذ لم يتيسر لي الاطّلاع على الكتب المنطقيَّةِ إلا على شرح الرسالةِ الشَّمسية، فاستخرجتُ منه المسائل على حسب ذهني ودهائي، واستفدت منه الفوائد على قدْرِ فهمي وذكائي، فكتبتُها في هذا الكتاب؛ تبصرةً لمن تبصر، وتذكرةً لمن أراد أن يتذكَّر .
الدسوقي
قَوْلُهُ : وَأَسْتَغْفِرُ اللهَ) جملةٌ ماضويّةٌ عطفٌ على ما قَبْلَها ؛ أي: واطلبُ مِنَ اللهِ
أن يغفر لي من أجل سهوي. وفيه : أَنَّ السَّهو ليس ذنباً حتَّى يُطلب مغفرته، وأُجِيبُ : بأنَّ قولَه : مِن سَهوي
على حذف مضاف ؛ أي: مِن مُسبّب سهوي، وهو التقصيرُ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَرِجْلِي) أي : مَشْيِي؛ عطفٌ على البضاعة، ويحتمل أَنَّه معطوفٌ على
قلة؛ أي: وَبِأَنِّي ماشي في ذلك الميدان، وَلَسْتُ بِفَارس في مضمار . (قَوْلُهُ : فِي مِضْمَارِ) المضمارُ : الموضعُ تضمرُ فيهِ الخيل؛ أي: تركب ويتسابق فيه الفرسان .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَدَهَائِي) الدَّهاءُ : - بفتحِ الدَّالِ المهملة ـ جودة الرأي، كما في القاموس، والمراد هنا : الرأي.
قبله .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَذَكَائِي) الذكاء : سرعة الفهم والمراد به هنا : الفهم، فهو مُرادِفٌ لِمَا
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا عَلَى شَرْحِ الرِّسَالَةِ المرادُ : مَا يتعلَّق بشرح كلامِهَا، فَلَا يُنافي أَنَّهُ ذكر في بعض المباحث شيئاً مِنَ حاشيةِ السَّيِّدِ كَما نبهنا على ذلك هناك، وإلى هنا انتهى بِنَا الكلام، والحمدُ للهِ في المبدأ والختام، وقد كنتُ وصلَتْ بي الكتابة إلى برهان الخلف، ثمَّ توجَّهْتُ إلى الإسكندرية، وفيها وقع الإتمام، ولم أستصحب
Conclusion. Those who deserved the rank of authorship and not among those who by truth attain the virtue of authorship, yet if this my composition finds acceptance in the Khaganian presence, it would become famous in the regions like the spread of the east wind and the west wind. Then the hope from the generosities of peers and the virtues of friends is that they overlook what is in it of forgetfulness and error with pardon and forgiveness, and if they come across clear error, let them cover it with correction. Dassuqi: And the 'Anqa' is a very large bird, and the intended meaning is that there is a very great difference between me and those who deserved the rank of authorship. His saying: 'So I do not know' means: I do not consider myself... etc., it is a justification for what came before. (His saying: 'and not among those who by truth attain the virtue of authorship') the addition of 'virtue' to what follows is explicative, and likewise the addition of 'rank' to what follows. Wind. (His saying: 'the Khaganian') attribution to Khagan, which is a title for the kings of the Turks. (His saying: 'the east wind') a wind whose origin is from the rising of the Pleiades to the Big Dipper, and 'the west wind' is like the form of the east wind because it faces the opposite wind, or because it faces the door of the Kaaba. From al-Qamus. Thus the conjunction of 'west wind' with 'east wind' is synonymous. (His saying: 'Then the hoped') from 'hope', which is expectation; i.e., then the hoped-for, and 'peers' is plural of 'peer', who is equal in age, and the intended here is the one who shares with him in knowledge. (And 'friends') its origin is 'khilal', plural of 'khalil', its final lam was replaced with a nun, and the intended by 'friends' is loved ones. (His saying: 'of what is in it of forgetfulness') i.e., the cause of forgetfulness and error. (His saying: 'let them cover it with correction') i.e., by writing in its margin: its correct form is such, or the intended meaning is such, not correction by erasing it and writing what appears to be correct in its place; for perhaps the erased is the actual correct one. Al-Fahd: And how many a critic of a sound statement, and its affliction is from the... Al-Attar: May Allah reward well whoever contemplates my work and meets what is in it of error with pardon and corrects what I erred in with his grace and intelligence, and I seek forgiveness from Allah for my forgetfulness, for I confess the scarcity of my goods and my foot in the arena of that craft; since I did not have access to the books of logic except to the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. I extracted from it the issues according to my mind and intelligence, and I benefited from it the benefits to the extent of my understanding and quickness, so I wrote them in this book as a lesson for whoever takes heed and a reminder for whoever wants to remember. Dassuqi: His saying: 'And I seek forgiveness from Allah' is a past tense sentence coordinated with what precedes it; i.e., I ask Allah to forgive me for my forgetfulness. In it there is [an issue]: forgetfulness is not a sin such that its forgiveness is sought. But it is answered: that his saying 'from my forgetfulness' involves a deleted genitive; i.e., from the cause of my forgetfulness, which is shortcoming. So reflect. (His saying: 'and my foot') i.e., my walking; coordinated with 'scarcity of goods', and it is possible that it is coordinated with 'scarcity'; i.e., and that I am walking in that field, not a horseman in the race-track. (His saying: 'in the arena') the arena is the place where horses are trained; i.e., they ride and race in it. (His saying: 'and my intelligence') intelligence is sound opinion, as in al-Qamus, and the intended here is opinion. (His saying: 'and my quickness') quickness is speed of understanding, and the intended here is understanding, so it is synonymous with what precedes. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'except on the commentary on al-Risala') the intended is what pertains to the explanation of its speech, so it does not contradict that he mentioned in some discussions something from the Hashiya of al-Sayyid, as we have pointed out there. And here our speech ends, and praise be to Allah in the beginning and the end. And I had reached in writing to the proof of al-Khalf (reductio ad absurdum), then I headed to Alexandria, and there the completion occurred, and I did not bring with me...
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section (khatima) of the author's work, accompanied by the commentary of al-Dassuqi. The author humbly distances himself from those who have attained the rank of authorship, comparing himself to a lowly bird versus the mythical 'Anqa'. He expresses hope that his peers and friends will overlook his errors and correct his mistakes gently. Dassuqi explains the metaphors: 'Anqa' symbolizes a vast difference; 'east wind' and 'west wind' represent widespread fame; 'peers' are fellow scholars; 'friends' are loved ones. The author then directly addresses the reader, asking for pardon and correction. He confesses his limited resources, stating he only had access to one commentary (on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya) and compiled his work based on his own understanding. Dassuqi clarifies that seeking forgiveness for forgetfulness is appropriate because it stems from shortcoming. The author describes himself as a pedestrian in the arena of logic, not a horseman, and notes that his 'intelligence' and 'quickness' refer to his opinion and understanding. Dassuqi finally notes that the author's reference to 'only the commentary' does not preclude his use of other sources like al-Sayyid's Hashiya. The passage ends with the author's report that he had reached the proof of al-Khalf before traveling to Alexandria to complete the work.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of an Islamic scholarly work is a space for humility, acknowledgment of limits, and an invitation for collective correction.
